## Assistant (2026-04-19 16:04:20)

_Thought for 6m 13s_

Yes. I think the next honest attack is the \(T_v\) analogue, and I would formulate it this way.

The right theorem is not just “\(T_v\not\equiv 0\).” It is:

> **Finite-order isolated \(T_v=0\) boundaries are locally sterile for lifted 4-point birth, provided \(K_v\neq 0\) at the boundary.**

That extra \(K_v\neq 0\) is important. It means the lower triple
\[
1,\quad x,\quad \sigma_v
\]
is already locally ECT, so the 4-point problem is genuinely the next-order extension of the 3-point problem.

Here is the clean setup.

On a connected \(v_5\neq 0\) patch, write
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\qquad
\sigma(h):=\sigma_v(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)\,v_5(h)},\qquad
y(h):=\frac{u_5(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
Then the lifted space curve is
\[
Q_v(h)=(y(h),x(h),\sigma(h)).
\]

A 4-point coplanarity configuration is exactly the vanishing of
\[
E_v(s,t,u,w):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & y(s) & x(s) & \sigma(s)\\
1 & y(t) & x(t) & \sigma(t)\\
1 & y(u) & x(u) & \sigma(u)\\
1 & y(w) & x(w) & \sigma(w)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Now assume \(M\neq 0\), so \(x\) is a local coordinate. Rewrite everything in \(x\)-coordinate:
\[
y=Y(x),\qquad \sigma=S(x).
\]
Then
\[
T_v=W_x(1,Y,x,S).
\]

Since
\[
W_x(1,x,S)=S''=K_v
\]
up to the harmless change of parameter factor, the assumption \(K_v(h_b)\neq 0\) means the triple
\[
1,\quad x,\quad S(x)
\]
is locally ECT near the boundary.

That is the key observation.

At that point, the right object is the third-order differential operator whose kernel is exactly \(\operatorname{span}\{1,x,S\}\):
\[
\mathcal L[g]
:=
\frac{W_x(1,x,S,g)}{W_x(1,x,S)}
=
\frac{W_x(1,x,S,g)}{S''}.
\]
A direct calculation gives
\[
W_x(1,x,S,g)=S''\,g'''-S'''\,g'',
\]
so
\[
\mathcal L[g]
=
g'''-\frac{S'''}{S''}g''.
\]

In particular,
\[
\mathcal L[Y]
=
\frac{W_x(1,x,S,Y)}{S''}
=
\pm\,\frac{T_v}{K_v}.
\]

So \(T_v\) is exactly the forcing term for the quotient operator \(\mathcal L\) after you normalize by the lower ECT triple.

That is the right reduction.

Now fix three same-side points
\[
x_1<x_2<x_3<0
\]
near the boundary, and let \(L_{123}\in\operatorname{span}\{1,x,S\}\) be the unique interpolant satisfying
\[
L_{123}(x_i)=Y(x_i),\qquad i=1,2,3.
\]
Set
\[
G_{123}(x):=Y(x)-L_{123}(x).
\]
Then:
- \(G_{123}(x_1)=G_{123}(x_2)=G_{123}(x_3)=0\),
- and
\[
\mathcal L[G_{123}]=\mathcal L[Y]=\pm\,\frac{T_v}{K_v}.
\]

Also, by Cramer’s rule, the coplanarity determinant is zero exactly when
\[
G_{123}(x_4)=0.
\]
So the local 4-point birth problem is now:

> can a solution of \(\mathcal L G = \pm T_v/K_v\) with three prescribed same-side zeros pick up a new opposite-side zero near a finite-order boundary point of the forcing?

That is now a genuine 1-variable ODE / generalized divided-difference problem.

I think the cleanest way to finish it is to use the standard disconjugate/ECT machinery for the triple \((1,x,S)\). Concretely:

- because \(S''\neq 0\) near the boundary, the operator \(\mathcal L\) is disconjugate there;
- for each fixed triple \(x_1<x_2<x_3\), the remainder \(G_{123}\) has a Green-kernel representation
  \[
  G_{123}(x)=\int K_{123}(x,\xi)\,\mathcal L[Y](\xi)\,d\xi,
  \]
  where the Green kernel has fixed sign on each side;
- if \(T_v/K_v\) has even vanishing order at the boundary, it has fixed sign across the boundary, so \(G_{123}\) cannot pick up an opposite-side zero;
- if \(T_v/K_v\) has odd vanishing order, then the corresponding generalized divided difference is strictly monotone across the boundary, so there is at most one opposite-side zero.

That is the exact order-4 analogue of the divided-difference proof for the \(W\)- and \(K_v\)-theorems.

So the theorem I think is true is:

Let \(h_b\) satisfy
\[
c(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad M(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad v_5(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad K_v(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad T_v(h_b)=0,
\]
and assume
\[
T_v(h)=(h-h_b)^r\widetilde T_v(h),\qquad \widetilde T_v(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Then after shrinking to a small neighborhood of \(h_b\):

- if \(r\) is even, no opposite-side 4-point coplanar configuration of \(Q_v\) can be born there;
- if \(r\) is odd, at most one opposite-side 4-point coplanar branch can cross there.

That would be the lifted 4-point sterility theorem.

Now the part where I want to be careful:

I think this is **very plausible and likely correct**, but I would not call it fully closed from the current draft **unless** you are willing to explicitly invoke standard generalized divided-difference / disconjugate-ODE facts for ECT systems of order \(3\).

Why I’m stopping one step short of “done”:

For \(K_v\), we were still in the ordinary planar divided-difference setting, so the proof could be written directly with classical second divided differences.

For \(T_v\), the natural proof is no longer ordinary divided differences of a single graph \(f\). It is the order-4 interpolation remainder for the ECT triple \((1,x,S)\). That is standard territory, but it is a different layer of machinery.

So the cleanest honest attack is:

1. define \(\mathcal L = W(1,x,S,\cdot)/W(1,x,S)\);
2. observe \(\mathcal L[Y]=\pm T_v/K_v\);
3. formulate the 4-point coplanarity remainder \(G_{123}\);
4. invoke the standard sign/monotonicity theory for the Green kernel or generalized third divided differences of the disconjugate operator \(\mathcal L\).

That would close the local finite-order \(T_v\) boundary story.

And once that is in place, the lifted finite-core picture becomes very parallel to the new planar \(W=0\) picture:

- \(K_v\)-boundaries are locally sterile for lifted 3-point birth,
- \(T_v\)-boundaries are locally sterile for lifted 4-point birth,
- so the remaining lifted obstruction is again **global recurrence / re-entry**, not local birth.

That is the cleanest route I see from the current draft.
## User (2026-04-19 16:05:23)

attack

## Assistant (2026-04-19 16:07:20)

_Thought for 1m 54s_

Yes. This one closes more cleanly than I first thought.

The key extra observation is that once \(K_v\neq 0\), the order-4 problem reduces to a **disconjugate third-order operator**, and then ordinary Rolle counting is enough.

Here is the clean theorem.

Let
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\qquad
S(x(h)):=\sigma_v(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)\,v_5(h)},\qquad
Y(x(h)):=\frac{u_5(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
On a \(v_5\neq 0\) patch with \(M\neq 0\), \(x\) is a valid local coordinate.

Define the lifted coplanarity determinant
\[
E_v(s,t,u,w):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & Y(x(s)) & x(s) & S(x(s))\\
1 & Y(x(t)) & x(t) & S(x(t))\\
1 & Y(x(u)) & x(u) & S(x(u))\\
1 & Y(x(w)) & x(w) & S(x(w))
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Also define
\[
T_v=W_x(1,Y,x,S).
\]

Since
\[
W_x(1,x,S)=S''(x),
\]
the hypothesis \(K_v(h_b)\neq 0\) is exactly the statement that
\[
S''(0)\neq 0
\]
after centering at \(x(h_b)=0\).

Now define the normalized third-order operator
\[
\mathcal L[g]:=\frac{W_x(1,x,S,g)}{S''}.
\]
A direct calculation gives
\[
W_x(1,x,S,g)=S''g'''-S'''g'',
\]
hence
\[
\mathcal L[g]=g'''-\frac{S'''}{S''}g''.
\]
More importantly, its zero set is the same as that of
\[
\widetilde{\mathcal L}[g]:=\frac{1}{S''}\mathcal L[g]
=
\frac{d}{dx}\!\left(\frac{g''}{S''}\right).
\]

That is the real simplification.

Because \(S''\) has fixed nonzero sign near the boundary, \(\widetilde{\mathcal L}\) has the Pólya-type factorization
\[
\widetilde{\mathcal L}
=
D\circ \Bigl(\frac{1}{S''}\Bigr)\circ D\circ D,
\]
up to the harmless positive multiplier \(1/S''\). So ordinary Rolle counting applies:

- if \(g\) has \(m\) zeros, then \(g'\) has at least \(m-1\),
- \(g''\) has at least \(m-2\),
- \(g''/S''\) has the same zeros as \(g''\),
- \(\widetilde{\mathcal L}[g]\) has at least \(m-3\) zeros.

So for this operator, **\(m\) zeros of \(g\) force at least \(m-3\) zeros of \(\mathcal L[g]\)**.

That is exactly what we need.

Now fix three same-side points
\[
x_1<x_2<x_3<0
\]
near \(0\). Let \(L_{123}\in\operatorname{span}\{1,x,S\}\) be the unique interpolant satisfying
\[
L_{123}(x_i)=Y(x_i),\qquad i=1,2,3,
\]
and set
\[
G_{123}(x):=Y(x)-L_{123}(x).
\]
Then
\[
G_{123}(x_1)=G_{123}(x_2)=G_{123}(x_3)=0,
\]
and since \(L_{123}\in\ker \mathcal L\),
\[
\mathcal L[G_{123}]=\mathcal L[Y].
\]

But
\[
\mathcal L[Y]=\frac{W_x(1,x,S,Y)}{S''}
=
\pm\,\frac{T_v}{K_v},
\]
up to the harmless sign coming from column order. So the forcing term is exactly \(T_v/K_v\).

Also, by Cramer’s rule,
\[
E_v(x_1,x_2,x_3,u)=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
G_{123}(u)=0.
\]
So local 4-point coplanarity is equivalent to an extra zero of \(G_{123}\).

Now assume
\[
T_v(h)=(h-h_b)^r\widetilde T_v(h),
\qquad
\widetilde T_v(h_b)\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
K_v(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Since \(x'(h_b)\neq 0\), this is equivalent in the \(x\)-coordinate to
\[
\mathcal L[Y](x)=\frac{T_v}{K_v}=x^r\widetilde q(x),
\qquad
\widetilde q(0)\neq 0.
\]

So:

- if \(r\) is even, \(\mathcal L[Y]\) has fixed sign near \(0\), hence no zero there;
- if \(r\) is odd, \(\mathcal L[Y]\) has exactly one simple sign change at \(0\), hence at most one zero there.

Now apply the zero-counting rule above to \(G_{123}\).

If \(r\) is even and \(G_{123}\) had an extra opposite-side zero, then it would have \(4\) zeros total, so \(\mathcal L[G_{123}]\) would have at least \(1\) zero. But
\[
\mathcal L[G_{123}]=\mathcal L[Y]
\]
has no zero. Contradiction.

If \(r\) is odd and \(G_{123}\) had two extra opposite-side zeros, then it would have \(5\) zeros total, so \(\mathcal L[G_{123}]\) would have at least \(2\) zeros. But \(\mathcal L[Y]\) has at most one zero. Contradiction.

So we get:

### Finite-order \(T_v=0\) sterility theorem
Assume
\[
c(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad M(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad v_5(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad K_v(h_b)\neq 0,
\qquad T_v(h_b)=0,
\]
and \(T_v\) has finite vanishing order \(r\ge 1\) at \(h_b\). Then, after shrinking to a small neighborhood of \(h_b\):

- if \(r\) is even, no opposite-side 4-point coplanar configuration of the lifted curve \(Q_v\) can be born there;
- if \(r\) is odd, at most one opposite-side 4-point coplanar branch can cross there.

Equivalently: **finite-order isolated \(T_v=0\) boundaries are locally sterile for lifted 4-point birth, provided \(K_v\neq 0\) at the boundary.**

That is the right lifted analogue of the new finite-order \(W=0\) sterility theorem.

So the local finite-order picture is now very clean:

- finite-order isolated \(K_v=0\) boundaries are locally sterile for lifted 3-point birth;
- finite-order isolated \(T_v=0\) boundaries are locally sterile for lifted 4-point birth, as long as \(K_v\neq 0\) there.

What remains after that is exactly the same kind of thing your new \(W=0\) section identified on the older planar side:

- possible **infinite-order flatness**, and
- **global recurrence / re-entry**, not local birth.

This is a real advance. It means the lifted finite-core endgame is no longer blocked by local finite-order boundary creation.

The next honest thing to attack, after this, is the mixed boundary case
\[
K_v(h_b)=T_v(h_b)=0,
\]
because that is the only local finite-order configuration not covered by the theorem above. That is the lifted analogue of a higher-codimension turning/inflection coincidence, and it is probably where the remaining local subtlety lives.
## User (2026-04-19 16:08:22)

attack

## Assistant (2026-04-19 16:13:22)

_Thought for 4m 56s_

Yes. And the attack gives a real answer, but it is not the one we were hoping for.

The mixed boundary case
\[
K_v(h_b)=0,\qquad T_v(h_b)=0
\]
is **not** just a harder version of the \(K_v\neq 0\) case. It is genuinely different, and the local-sterility strategy can fail there.

Here is the clean reduction.

Work on a \(v_5\neq 0\) patch and use
\[
x(h)=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}
\]
as local coordinate, with \(x(h_b)=0\). Write
\[
S(x):=\sigma_v(h)=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)\,v_5(h)},
\qquad
Y(x):=\frac{u_5(h)}{c(h)}.
\]

Because \(K_v\) is the curvature numerator of \((x,S)\), the hypothesis that \(K_v\) has finite vanishing order means that after subtracting the tangent line at \(0\),
\[
S(x)=a_m x^m+O(x^{m+1}),
\qquad a_m\neq 0,
\qquad m\ge 3.
\]
Indeed \(m-2\) is exactly the vanishing order of \(K_v\).

Now use the fact that the 4-point coplanarity determinant is unchanged if we replace \(Y\) by
\[
Y-(A+Bx+CS),
\]
because that is just a column operation on the determinant
\[
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & Y & x & S
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
So we may affine-normalize \(Y\) modulo \(\operatorname{span}\{1,x,S\}\) and arrange
\[
Y(x)=b_n x^n+O(x^{n+1}),
\qquad b_n\neq 0,
\qquad n\notin\{0,1,m\}.
\]

This is the correct mixed-boundary normal form:
\[
Q_v(x)\sim (x^n,\ x,\ x^m).
\]

Now the key identities are immediate.

First,
\[
K_v \sim a_m m(m-1)x^{m-2}.
\]

Second,
\[
T_v=W_x(1,Y,x,S)
\]
has leading term
\[
T_v(x)
=
-a_m b_n\,m(m-1)n(n-1)(m-n)\,x^{m+n-5}
+O(x^{m+n-4}).
\]
So once the affine normalization has removed the \(x^m\)-part of \(Y\), the mixed condition \(T_v(h_b)=0\) is automatic simply because \(m+n-5>0\). In other words, the mixed boundary is exactly the regime where both the lifted planar curvature and the lifted torsion vanish to positive order.

Now look at the local 4-point coplanarity determinant. For four nearby nodes
\[
x_j=\varepsilon \xi_j,
\]
we get
\[
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & Y(x_1) & x_1 & S(x_1)\\
1 & Y(x_2) & x_2 & S(x_2)\\
1 & Y(x_3) & x_3 & S(x_3)\\
1 & Y(x_4) & x_4 & S(x_4)
\end{pmatrix}
=
\varepsilon^{m+n+1}a_m b_n\,
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & \xi_1^n & \xi_1 & \xi_1^m\\
1 & \xi_2^n & \xi_2 & \xi_2^m\\
1 & \xi_3^n & \xi_3 & \xi_3^m\\
1 & \xi_4^n & \xi_4 & \xi_4^m
\end{pmatrix}
+O(\varepsilon^{m+n+2}).
\]
After reordering columns, this is the generalized Vandermonde for exponents
\[
0,\ 1,\ m,\ n.
\]

So the mixed boundary problem reduces to a **pure finite-dimensional model problem**:

> can the generalized Vandermonde
> \[
> \mathfrak V_{m,n}(\xi_1,\xi_2,\xi_3,\xi_4)
> :=
> \det\!\begin{pmatrix}
> 1 & \xi_1 & \xi_1^m & \xi_1^n\\
> 1 & \xi_2 & \xi_2^m & \xi_2^n\\
> 1 & \xi_3 & \xi_3^m & \xi_3^n\\
> 1 & \xi_4 & \xi_4^m & \xi_4^n
> \end{pmatrix}
> \]
> vanish for three negative nodes and one positive node?

And here is the crucial point:

**yes, it can.**

A concrete example is
\[
(m,n)=(4,2).
\]
Then
\[
\mathfrak V_{4,2}(\xi_1,\xi_2,\xi_3,\xi_4)
=
\prod_{i<j}(\xi_j-\xi_i)\,(\xi_1+\xi_2+\xi_3+\xi_4).
\]
So if \(\xi_1,\xi_2,\xi_3<0<\xi_4\) and
\[
\xi_4=-(\xi_1+\xi_2+\xi_3),
\]
the leading determinant vanishes.

That means the mixed normal form can support a local opposite-side coplanar quadruple at leading order. So the finite-order local-sterility theorem that worked for \(W\), \(K_v\), and for \(T_v\) under \(K_v\neq 0\), **cannot hold in this general mixed case**.

That is the real attack result.

So the mixed case is not something to kill by the same local first-zero argument. It is a genuine codimension-two obstruction, and the correct conclusion is:

1. The \(K_v\neq 0\) local theory is fine.
2. The mixed case \(K_v=T_v=0\) reduces to the affine-normal monomial model
   \[
   (Y,x,S)\sim (x^n,x,x^m).
   \]
3. In that model, local 4-point coplanarity can occur.
4. Therefore the paper cannot close the lifted 4-point story by a blanket finite-order local-sterility theorem at mixed boundaries.

What does that suggest as the next route?

The most promising route is no longer “prove mixed local sterility.” It is to extract a **new invariant of the mixed normal form** and show the actual one-pair zeta geometry cannot realize the dangerous generalized-Vandermonde zero patterns.

Concretely, the next object to isolate is:

- the order \(m\) of the first nonzero term of the normalized \(S\)-jet,
- the order \(n\) of the first nonzero term of the normalized \(Y\)-jet modulo \(\operatorname{span}\{1,x,S\}\),
- and the leading generalized-Vandermonde / Schur factor for \((m,n)\).

That turns the mixed boundary into a sharply finite-dimensional obstruction.

So the honest state after this attack is:

> the mixed case does not collapse by the same local sterility mechanism; instead it reduces to a codimension-two affine normal form whose leading 4-point determinant is a generalized Vandermonde, and that model can genuinely admit local opposite-side coplanar quadruples.

That is a useful result, because it tells you exactly where the current route stops and what new ingredient is actually needed.
## User (2026-04-19 16:15:23)

ok please write a full summary of everything new you've learned since the most recent paper draft

## Assistant (2026-04-19 16:16:28)

_Thought for 1m 2s_

Here is the cleanest full summary of what I’ve learned since the latest draft.

The biggest correction is structural: the old “minimal bad core rigidity” picture was aimed at the wrong object for \(k\ge 3\). For two points, vanishing forces proportionality. For three or more points, it does not force a common line; it forces a **projective/affine circuit**. That changes the finite-core endgame.

What is now solid in the draft itself is this.

At quintic order, the right homogeneous datum is
\[
V(h)=(c(h),u_5(h),v_5(h)),
\qquad
\Xi(h)=\left(\frac{u_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}\right).
\]
In the defect-free order-\(\le 5\) model, a bad finite core gives
\[
\sum_j a_j V(h_j)=0,
\]
equivalently an affine dependence among the \(\Xi(h_j)\). So minimal defect-free bad cores have size at most \(4\):
two points give coincidence,
three points give a collinear triple,
four points give an affine circuit in the plane.
That is the correct quintic reduction.

At order \(7\), on a \(v_5\neq 0\) patch, the right lifted coordinates are
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad
y=\frac{u_5}{c},\qquad
\sigma_v=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5},
\qquad
Q_v=(y,x,\sigma_v).
\]
In the defect-free order-\(\le 7\) model, the bad-core relation becomes affine dependence of the lifted points \(Q_v(h_j)\). So:
two points force coincidence on \(Q_v\),
three force collinearity on \(Q_v\),
four force coplanarity on \(Q_v\).
The explicit \(4\times 4\) determinant form for coplanarity is now on the page, and there is a parallel \(u_5\neq 0\) patch story.

There are also two key one-pair facts now isolated.

First,
\[
1,\quad \frac{u_5}{c},\quad \frac{v_5}{c}
\]
are linearly independent on any connected interval where \(c\neq 0\) and \(M\neq 0\). The proof uses the tagged quintic witness: the monomial \(\kappa\eta_2 q^{(5)}\) occurs in \(u_5\), but not in \(v_5\) and not in \(c\).

Second,
\[
\sigma_v=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}
\]
is not affine in
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c}
\]
on a \(v_5\neq 0\) patch in the quintic-generic region, because such an affine relation would force \(\Delta_7\) to be built from lower data only and would kill the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice.

From there the lifted one-pair geometry becomes very clear.

Define the lifted curvature numerator \(K_v\) for the planar curve
\[
\Lambda_v=(x,\sigma_v).
\]
It satisfies an exact bridge formula, and \(K_v\not\equiv 0\). So on subintervals where \(K_v\) has fixed nonzero sign, the triple
\[
1,\ x,\ \sigma_v
\]
is ECT, and the defect-free local 3-point branch is excluded there.

Define the lifted order-7 Wronskian
\[
T_v=
W_h\!\left(1,\frac{u_5}{c},\frac{v_5}{c},\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}\right).
\]
This is also not identically zero. So on subintervals where \(T_v\) has fixed nonzero sign, the quadruple
\[
1,\ \frac{u_5}{c},\ \frac{v_5}{c},\ \frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}
\]
is ECT, and the defect-free local 4-point branch is excluded there.

Those are now in the paper. The more important things I learned after that, and which are not yet fully reflected in the TeX, are these.

The first is that the new \(W=0\) work in the paper is more than cleanup. It gives the right template for the lifted story. On the old planar side, you now know:
open-interval \(W\equiv 0\) is gone,
finite-order isolated \(W=0\) boundaries are locally sterile for pair-pair birth,
so the remaining obstruction is not local birth but **global recurrence / re-entry**.
That same conceptual template now applies to the lifted determinants \(K_v\) and \(T_v\).

The next concrete theorem I pushed is:

If \(K_v(h_b)=0\) has finite order at an isolated boundary point, with
\[
c(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad M(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad v_5(h_b)\neq 0,
\]
then that boundary is locally sterile for lifted 3-point birth.
Using \(x=v_5/c\) as coordinate and writing \(\sigma_v=f(x)\), the determinant for three lifted points is governed by the second divided difference of \(f\), and finite-order vanishing of \(K_v\) is exactly finite-order vanishing of \(f''\). Then the same even/odd parity argument as in the new \(W=0\) theorem goes through:
even order gives no opposite-side birth,
odd order gives at most one crossing branch.
So finite-order isolated \(K_v=0\) boundaries do not create new lifted 3-point configurations.

I also pushed the analogous theorem for \(T_v\), but with an important hypothesis:
it works **provided \(K_v\neq 0\) at the boundary**.

In \(x\)-coordinates, write
\[
Y(x)=\frac{u_5}{c},\qquad S(x)=\sigma_v.
\]
Then
\[
T_v=W_x(1,Y,x,S).
\]
If \(K_v\neq 0\), equivalently \(S''\neq 0\), the lower triple \((1,x,S)\) is already disconjugate, and the order-4 problem reduces to the normalized third-order operator
\[
\mathcal L[g]=\frac{W_x(1,x,S,g)}{S''}.
\]
A solution with three same-side interpolation zeros can only pick up opposite-side zeros according to the zero pattern of \(\mathcal L[g]\), i.e. according to \(T_v/K_v\). From ordinary Rolle counting after factorization, you get:
finite-order isolated \(T_v=0\) with \(K_v\neq 0\) is locally sterile for lifted 4-point birth in the same parity sense:
even order gives no opposite-side birth,
odd order gives at most one crossing branch.

This is a real gain. It means the lifted local finite-order story is under control except for one remaining local case.

And that remaining local case turned out to be the biggest new thing I learned:

The mixed boundary
\[
K_v(h_b)=0,\qquad T_v(h_b)=0
\]
is genuinely different, and the blanket local-sterility strategy fails there.

After affine normalization at the boundary, the lifted curve takes the monomial normal form
\[
Q_v(x)\sim (x^n,\ x,\ x^m),
\]
where \(m\ge 3\) is the first nonzero order in the normalized \(S\)-jet and \(n\) is the first nonzero order in the normalized \(Y\)-jet modulo \(\operatorname{span}\{1,x,S\}\).

In that mixed normal form, the 4-point coplanarity determinant has leading term equal to a generalized Vandermonde for exponents
\[
0,\ 1,\ m,\ n.
\]
And crucially, that leading generalized Vandermonde can vanish for three negative nodes and one positive node. A concrete example is \((m,n)=(4,2)\), where the factorization includes
\[
\xi_1+\xi_2+\xi_3+\xi_4,
\]
so an opposite-side quadruple can occur at leading order.

That means a blanket theorem saying “finite-order isolated \(T_v=0\) boundaries are locally sterile” is false in the mixed case. The mixed case is a genuine codimension-two obstruction, not something you kill by the same first-contact argument.

This is probably the single most important new thing from the recent push, because it tells us exactly where the current route stops.

So the local lifted picture now looks like this:

Finite-order \(K_v=0\) boundaries are locally sterile for lifted 3-point birth.

Finite-order \(T_v=0\) boundaries with \(K_v\neq 0\) are locally sterile for lifted 4-point birth.

The mixed case \(K_v=T_v=0\) does not collapse by the same mechanism; it reduces to a codimension-two affine normal form whose leading 4-point determinant is a generalized Vandermonde, and that model can genuinely admit opposite-side coplanar quadruples.

That pushes the remaining lifted obstruction into the same broad category as the new \(W=0\) story, but with one extra codimension-two branch:
the issues are no longer generic local finite-order birth; they are now
global recurrence / re-entry,
possible infinite-order flatness,
and mixed normal-form obstructions.

The practical consequence is that the cleanest route forward is now much sharper than before.

The interaction-defect extraction
\[
E_{\ge2}^{(3)},\quad E_{\ge2}^{(5)},\quad \overline E_{\ge2}^{(7)}
\]
is still open, but that is algebraic packaging.

On the geometric side, the next real targets are:
first, formalize the \(K_v\)-sterility theorem;
second, formalize the \(T_v\)-sterility theorem under \(K_v\neq 0\);
third, isolate the mixed normal-form invariants \((m,n)\) and the corresponding generalized-Vandermonde / Schur factor, and ask whether the actual one-pair zeta geometry can realize the dangerous mixed patterns.

That is the main new map.

So the cleanest one-paragraph summary is:

Since the latest draft, the finite-core story has been correctly reframed from “common leading line” to affine/projective circuits; the order-7 lifted curve on a \(v_5\)-patch is now the right object; its local 3-point and 4-point branches are controlled by the new determinants \(K_v\) and \(T_v\); finite-order local birth is ruled out for \(K_v\), and for \(T_v\) when \(K_v\neq 0\); but the mixed case \(K_v=T_v=0\) survives and reduces to a genuine codimension-two monomial normal form with generalized-Vandermonde leading determinant, so the remaining problem is no longer generic local birth but recurrence, infinite-order flatness, and mixed normal-form exclusion.

_Thought for 19s_

This is good. It fits the current picture very well, and it gives a genuinely useful bridge that I did **not** have before: an exact/quantitative way to pass from a bad two-pair core to **near-coincidence of the strengthened invariant** \(\widehat\Psi\). 

My take is:

The algebraic core looks right. The formulas for
\[
\widehat\Psi(h)=\left(\frac{u_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)^2}\right)
\]
are exactly the sort of identities you want, and the third-coordinate computation is the nontrivial part; the determinant manipulation with a representative \(R\) of the septic quotient defect is the right way to package it. The nice point is that \(\det(R,A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f})\) is representative-independent, so the formula is canonical at the level needed for estimates. 

What this buys you is substantial. It turns the vague statement “bad two-pair cores should force coincidence of the strengthened invariant” into a precise theorem:
\[
|\widehat\Psi(h_1)-\widehat\Psi(h_2)|
\lesssim
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+|E_{12}^{(5)}|+|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|
\]
up to the harmless quadratic \( |E_{12}^{(3)}|^2 \) term on compact regions. That is exactly the right bridge between the algebraic two-pair section and the lifted \(v_5\)-patch geometry. 

I also agree with the next corollary in the note: on a good \(M\neq 0\) patch, the second coordinate
\[
x(h)=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}
\]
has derivative
\[
x'(h)=\frac{M(h)}{c(h)^2},
\]
so on a compact patch with \(c\) bounded away from \(0\) and \(M\) bounded away from \(0\), \(\widehat\Psi\) is locally Lipschitz-injective just from that second coordinate. Then the quantitative coincidence estimate forces
\[
|h_1-h_2|
\lesssim
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+|E_{12}^{(5)}|+|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|.
\]
That is a real theorem-strength reduction: bad two-pair cores on good patches must be defect-scale close. 

So I think the other chat has identified the right next **algebraic** theorem.

Where this now fits into the picture I built from the lifted \(K_v,T_v\) side is very clean:

First layer: the defect-thickened coincidence theorem kills the “far apart” regime quantitatively. If two bad points are separated on a good \(M\neq 0\) patch, the strengthened invariant separates them, so a bad two-pair core is impossible unless the interaction defects are correspondingly large. 

Second layer: the remaining regime is the **close-point** regime. That is exactly where the lifted local geometry belongs:
\[
\Lambda_v=(x,\sigma_v),\qquad
Q_v=(y,x,\sigma_v),
\]
with
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\quad y=\frac{u_5}{c},\quad \sigma_v=\frac{\Delta_7}{cv_5}.
\]
That is where the \(K_v\) and \(T_v\) machinery should attack first-contact / branching. So the two routes are not competing. They actually splice together perfectly: the other chat’s theorem handles quantitative reduction to close pairs, and the lifted determinants handle the local close-pair geometry. 

There are three caveats I would keep in view.

The first is that the theorem is only as good as the defect bookkeeping. You still need actual control of
\[
E_{12}^{(3)},\quad E_{12}^{(5)},\quad \overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}
\]
in the corrected finite-order model. The other chat is right that this is the next algebraic target; it does not come for free. 

The second is that the Lipschitz-injectivity corollary is patchwise. It works on compact regions with \(M\neq 0\) and \(c\neq 0\), but it does not by itself solve crossing of turning boundaries or recurrence across multiple intervals. That meshes with the new \(W=0\) story rather than replacing it. 

The third, and most important from my own recent work, is that this theorem does **not** eliminate the mixed local obstruction I found for the lifted 4-point problem. Even if the defect-thickened coincidence theorem pushes bad two-pair cores into a close regime, the close regime can still hit the mixed codimension-two boundary
\[
K_v=T_v=0,
\]
and in that mixed normal form the leading 4-point determinant is a generalized Vandermonde that can genuinely vanish. So this new quantitative route improves the endgame a lot, but it does not by itself remove the mixed local obstruction. It reduces the problem to it more cleanly.

So my clean verdict is:

The other chat’s work is real and valuable. It should probably be the next theorem on the algebraic side. It gives the missing quantitative bridge from bad two-pair cores to near-coincidence of \(\widehat\Psi\), and together with the lifted \(K_v,T_v\) section it gives a very sharp dichotomy:

either the defects are not small, or the bad pair is defect-scale close; and once it is close, the problem becomes a local lifted first-contact problem. 

The cleanest next attack, in order, is:

1. Prove the quantitative defect-thickened strengthened coincidence theorem exactly as the other chat sketched it. 

2. Deduce the compact \(M\neq 0\) patch corollary that bad two-pair cores are defect-scale close. 

3. Then attack the local close-point regime with the lifted \(K_v,T_v\) geometry, keeping in mind that the generic finite-order boundary cases are locally sterile, but the mixed case \(K_v=T_v=0\) remains the serious codimension-two local obstruction.

## User (2026-04-19 16:21:48)

here's what the other chat suggests

This is **very good**. It materially sharpens the finite/bad-core route, and I think it changes the roadmap in a useful way. The key gain is that it separates the lifted local problem into three regimes instead of treating all (T_v)-zeros the same:

* finite-order (K_v=0): locally sterile for lifted (3)-point birth;
* finite-order (T_v=0) with (K_v\neq 0): locally sterile for lifted (4)-point birth;
* mixed (K_v=T_v=0): genuinely different, with a codimension-two monomial normal form whose leading (4)-point determinant is a generalized Vandermonde and can admit opposite-side quadruples. 

That is a real structural improvement. It means the lifted route is no longer “generic local birth everywhere until proven otherwise.” Generic finite-order local birth is now mostly under control, and the remaining lifted obstruction is sharply localized to:

* global recurrence / re-entry,
* possible infinite-order flatness,
* and the mixed normal-form case (K_v=T_v=0). 

I think the most important new conclusion in that summary is exactly this:

> a blanket theorem “finite-order isolated (T_v=0) boundaries are locally sterile” is false; the mixed case is a genuine codimension-two obstruction.

That is the kind of negative result that saves a lot of wasted time. It tells us not to keep pushing a false universal local sterility theorem on the lifted side. 

So, strategically, I would now update the hierarchy of attacks like this.

First priority: **formalize the two lifted sterility theorems** that now look real.

Those are:

1. finite-order isolated (K_v=0) implies local sterility for lifted (3)-point birth;
2. finite-order isolated (T_v=0) with (K_v\neq 0) implies local sterility for lifted (4)-point birth. 

Those should become theorem-level statements in the paper, because they are exactly parallel to the new (W=0) story on the one-pair side and they cleanly remove generic local lifted birth as the main obstruction.

Second priority: **make the mixed case explicit as the remaining local lifted obstruction**.

I would not bury it in a remark. I would surface it as an audit/proposition-level statement:

* after affine normalization, the mixed boundary reduces to
  [
  Q_v(x)\sim (x^n,x,x^m),
  ]
* the leading coplanarity determinant is the generalized Vandermonde for exponents
  [
  0,1,m,n,
  ]
* and this model can genuinely admit opposite-side quadruples for some patterns, so the mixed case is not killed by the same first-contact argument. 

That tells the reader exactly where the local lifted route stops.

Third priority: **decide whether the actual zeta geometry can realize the dangerous mixed patterns** ((m,n)).

To me, this is now the cleanest next theorem target on the lifted side. The question is no longer generic local birth. It is:

> does the actual one-pair zeta geometry permit the mixed normal forms whose generalized Vandermonde admits opposite-side coplanar quadruples?

If the answer is no, the lifted route gets dramatically cleaner. If the answer is yes, then the mixed case becomes the one genuine local obstruction that the global endgame must carry.

Fourth priority: keep the **quantitative defect-thickened strengthened coincidence** route in parallel, but I would now treat it as the **global bridge**, not the immediate local attack.

That route is still valuable because it says bad two-pair cores force near-coincidence of the strengthened invariant (\widehat\Psi). But the new lifted summary tells us the local geometric side is now more sharply stratified than we previously thought. So I would use the quantitative coincidence theorem to force bad cores into the close-point regime, and then use the lifted stratification:

* generic close-point bad cores are killed by the (K_v)- and (T_v)-sterility theorems,
* only mixed (K_v=T_v=0) close-point cores remain.

That is a much cleaner endgame split than before. 

So my current ranking is:

1. write/prove the (K_v)-sterility theorem;
2. write/prove the (T_v)-sterility theorem under (K_v\neq 0);
3. isolate the mixed normal-form invariants ((m,n)) and test whether the dangerous patterns are realizable in the actual geometry;
4. in parallel, prove the quantitative defect-thickened strengthened coincidence theorem to force bad two-pair cores into this local lifted classification. 

That is now cleaner than the old one-pair (\beta)-route, because it replaces a vague global recurrence problem by a much more structured dichotomy: generic local lifted birth is gone, and the remaining local obstruction is a specific codimension-two mixed model.

So my bottom line is:

**Yes, this is a real advance, and it changes the route.**
The lifted finite-core story is no longer just “another possible approach.” It now gives the sharpest map of the remaining local obstructions, and I would lean into it.

on compact regions with the obvious denominator bounds. That is exactly the missing global reduction, because it turns a bad two-pair core into **near-coincidence of the strengthened invariant** rather than only defect-free coincidence. \lesssim

\]
meaning bad two-pair cores are defect-scale close. That is a real theorem-level payoff, not just a heuristic.  

So I agree with the other chat that this should now be the next **global bridge theorem**. Where I would sharpen the roadmap is this: the local lifted side is no longer a single “compact first-contact theorem for \(K_v,T_v\).” It is now a **stratified** local problem.

The right local hierarchy is:

- finite-order \(K_v=0\): locally sterile for lifted 3-point birth;
- finite-order \(T_v=0\) with \(K_v\neq 0\): locally sterile for lifted 4-point birth;
- mixed \(K_v=T_v=0\): genuinely different, and not killed by the same blanket local-sterility argument.

3. On the local lifted side, prove the two generic sterility theorems separately:
\(K_v\)-sterility for 3-point birth, and \(T_v\)-sterility for 4-point birth under \(K_v\neq 0\).

4. Surface the mixed case \(K_v=T_v=0\) as the real remaining local obstruction, not as an afterthought.

## 1. Replace the current remark “What remains in the two-pair rewrite”

Find this block:

```latex
\begin{remark}[What remains in the two-pair rewrite]
\label{rem:what-remains-two-pair-rewrite}
Proposition~\ref{prop:exact-strengthened-two-pair-coincidence} is the exact
defect-free order-$\le 7$ coincidence statement. What remains is to rewrite
the actual interaction germ $\mathcal I_{12}$ explicitly in the corrected
finite-order model and extract the corresponding defect terms
\[
E_{12}^{(3)}\in\mathbf C,
\qquad
E_{12}^{(5)}\in\mathfrak f,
\qquad
\overline E_{12}^{(7)}
\in
\mathfrak f/\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]
Once this is done, the same proof yields a defect-thickened coincidence
statement for $\widehat\Psi(h)$, replacing the older schematic language of
the cubic leading line $\ell_h$ and first residual class $\overline v_1(h)$.
\end{remark}
```

Replace it with this:

```latex
\begin{theorem}[Quantitative defect-thickened strengthened coincidence]
\label{thm:quantitative-defect-thickened-strengthened-coincidence}
Let \((a_1,h_1;a_2,h_2)\) be a normalized bad two-pair core, and write
\[
c_i:=c(h_i),
\qquad
A_{5,i}^{\mathfrak f}:=A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h_i)=u_{5,i}I+v_{5,i}S,
\qquad
\Delta_i:=\Delta_7(h_i).
\]
Assume
\[
a_1\neq 0,
\qquad
c_1c_2\neq 0.
\]
Suppose the cubic, quintic, and septic quotient defects are written as
\[
a_1c_1+a_2c_2+E_{12}^{(3)}=0,
\]
\[
a_1A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}+a_2A_{5,2}^{\mathfrak f}+E_{12}^{(5)}=0,
\]
and
\[
a_1[A_{7,1}^{\mathfrak f}]+a_2[A_{7,2}^{\mathfrak f}]
+\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}=0
\qquad\text{in}\qquad
\mathfrak f/\mathbf C A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}.
\]
Set
\[
\lambda:=-\frac{a_2}{a_1},
\qquad
e:=\frac{E_{12}^{(3)}}{a_1},
\qquad
E:=\frac{E_{12}^{(5)}}{a_1}=e_u I+e_v S.
\]
Choose any representative \(R\in\mathfrak f\) of the quotient class
\[
-\frac{1}{a_1}\,\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)},
\]
so that
\[
A_{7,1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\lambda A_{7,2}^{\mathfrak f}
+
R
+
\eta A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}
\]
for some \(\eta\in\mathbf C\). Then the strengthened invariant
\[
\widehat\Psi(h)=
\left(
\frac{u_5(h)}{c(h)},
\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)^2}
\right)
\]
satisfies the exact identities
\[
\frac{u_{5,1}}{c_1}-\frac{u_{5,2}}{c_2}
=
\frac{u_{5,2}e-c_2e_u}{c_1c_2},
\]
\[
\frac{v_{5,1}}{c_1}-\frac{v_{5,2}}{c_2}
=
\frac{v_{5,2}e-c_2e_v}{c_1c_2},
\]
and
\[
\frac{\Delta_1}{c_1^2}-\frac{\Delta_2}{c_2^2}
=
\frac{
-\lambda c_2^2\det(A_{7,2}^{\mathfrak f},E)
+
c_2^2\det(R,A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f})
+
\Delta_2(2\lambda c_2 e-e^2)
}{c_1^2c_2^2}.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The cubic defect identity gives
\[
c_1=\lambda c_2-e.
\]
The quintic defect identity gives
\[
A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}=\lambda A_{5,2}^{\mathfrak f}-E,
\]
hence
\[
u_{5,1}=\lambda u_{5,2}-e_u,
\qquad
v_{5,1}=\lambda v_{5,2}-e_v.
\]
Therefore
\[
\frac{u_{5,1}}{c_1}-\frac{u_{5,2}}{c_2}
=
\frac{u_{5,1}c_2-u_{5,2}c_1}{c_1c_2}
=
\frac{(\lambda u_{5,2}-e_u)c_2-u_{5,2}(\lambda c_2-e)}{c_1c_2}
=
\frac{u_{5,2}e-c_2e_u}{c_1c_2},
\]
and similarly
\[
\frac{v_{5,1}}{c_1}-\frac{v_{5,2}}{c_2}
=
\frac{v_{5,2}e-c_2e_v}{c_1c_2}.
\]

For the third coordinate, by the choice of \(R\) we have
\[
A_{7,1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\lambda A_{7,2}^{\mathfrak f}
+
R
+
\eta A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}
\]
for some \(\eta\in\mathbf C\). Using
\[
\Delta_i=\det(A_{7,i}^{\mathfrak f},A_{5,i}^{\mathfrak f}),
\]
we obtain
\[
\Delta_1
=
\det(A_{7,1}^{\mathfrak f},A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f})
=
\lambda\det(A_{7,2}^{\mathfrak f},A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f})
+
\det(R,A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}),
\]
since the \(\eta A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}\) term drops out. Substituting
\[
A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}=\lambda A_{5,2}^{\mathfrak f}-E
\]
gives
\[
\det(A_{7,2}^{\mathfrak f},A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f})
=
\lambda\Delta_2-\det(A_{7,2}^{\mathfrak f},E),
\]
hence
\[
\Delta_1-\lambda^2\Delta_2
=
-\lambda\det(A_{7,2}^{\mathfrak f},E)
+
\det(R,A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}).
\]

Also,
\[
c_1=\lambda c_2-e
\]
implies
\[
\lambda^2c_2^2-c_1^2=2\lambda c_2 e-e^2.
\]
Therefore
\[
\frac{\Delta_1}{c_1^2}-\frac{\Delta_2}{c_2^2}
=
\frac{c_2^2\Delta_1-c_1^2\Delta_2}{c_1^2c_2^2}
=
\frac{
c_2^2(\Delta_1-\lambda^2\Delta_2)+\Delta_2(\lambda^2c_2^2-c_1^2)
}{c_1^2c_2^2},
\]
which is exactly the claimed formula.

Finally, the expression
\[
\det(R,A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f})
\]
is independent of the chosen representative of the quotient defect, since
replacing \(R\) by \(R+\xi A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f}\) does not change the
determinant.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Bad two-pair cores are defect-scale close on good \(M\neq 0\) patches]
\label{cor:bad-two-pair-cores-defect-scale-close}
Let \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\) be a compact interval. Assume
\[
|a_1|\ge a_*>0
\]
for the normalized bad two-pair core under consideration. Then there exists a
constant \(C_J>0\) such that whenever \(h_1,h_2\in J\),
\[
|h_1-h_2|
\le
C_J\Bigl(|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|\Bigr).
\]
In particular, on good \(M\neq 0\) patches, bad two-pair cores force the two
parameters to be quantitatively close once the cubic/quintic interaction
defects are small.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
The second coordinate of \(\widehat\Psi\) is
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
x'(h)=\frac{M(h)}{c(h)^2}.
\]
Since \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), there exists \(\eta_J>0\) such that
\[
|x(h_1)-x(h_2)|\ge \eta_J\,|h_1-h_2|
\qquad
(h_1,h_2\in J).
\]
On the other hand, Theorem~\ref{thm:quantitative-defect-thickened-strengthened-coincidence}
gives the exact identity
\[
x(h_1)-x(h_2)
=
\frac{v_{5,2}e-c_2e_v}{c_1c_2},
\]
with
\[
e=\frac{E_{12}^{(3)}}{a_1},
\qquad
e_v=\text{\(S\)-coefficient of } \frac{E_{12}^{(5)}}{a_1}.
\]
Since \(J\) is compact and \(a_1\) is bounded away from \(0\), the right-hand
side is bounded by
\[
C'_J\Bigl(|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|\Bigr)
\]
for some constant \(C'_J\). Combining the two estimates yields the claim.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[What remains in the two-pair rewrite]
\label{rem:what-remains-two-pair-rewrite}
Theorem~\ref{thm:quantitative-defect-thickened-strengthened-coincidence}
supplies the correct algebraic bridge from the two-pair core to the
strengthened invariant \(\widehat\Psi\). In particular, on good \(M\neq 0\)
patches, bad two-pair cores are forced into a defect-scale close regime by
Corollary~\ref{cor:bad-two-pair-cores-defect-scale-close}.

What remains is to rewrite the actual interaction germ \(\mathcal I_{12}\)
explicitly in the corrected finite-order model and control the defect terms
\[
E_{12}^{(3)},
\qquad
E_{12}^{(5)},
\qquad
\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}.
\]
Once that is done, the finite-core endgame splits cleanly into two stages:
first force bad cores into the close-point regime through
\(\widehat\Psi\), then attack that local regime using the lifted \(v_5\)-patch
geometry and the determinants \(K_v\) and \(T_v\).
\end{remark}
```

This is the strongest thing I think is immediately theorem-ready.

## 2. Add a short WIP remark recording the new lifted stratification

Find this block:

```latex
\begin{remark}[Work in progress: interaction defects and zero-capture for the lifted order-$7$ geometry]
\label{rem:wip-lifted-order7-zero-capture}
...
\end{remark}
```

Immediately **after** it, insert this:

```latex
\begin{remark}[Work in progress: stratified lifted local picture]
\label{rem:wip-stratified-lifted-local-picture}
The lifted local obstruction now appears to split into three regimes.

First, finite-order isolated zeros of \(K_v\) should be locally sterile for
lifted \(3\)-point birth on \(v_5\neq 0\) patches, by the same
first-contact / divided-difference mechanism as in the finite-order
\(W=0\) theorem.

Second, finite-order isolated zeros of \(T_v\) with \(K_v\neq 0\) should be
locally sterile for lifted \(4\)-point birth, since on such patches the lower
triple
\[
1,\qquad \frac{v_5}{c},\qquad \frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}
\]
is already an ECT system and the order-\(4\) problem reduces to the next
disconjugate layer.

Third, the mixed case
\[
K_v=T_v=0
\]
is genuinely different. After affine normalization with
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c}
\]
as local coordinate, the lifted space curve is led to a monomial model
\[
Q_v(x)\sim (x^n,x,x^m),
\]
and the leading \(4\)-point coplanarity determinant becomes the generalized
Vandermonde for exponents
\[
0,\ 1,\ m,\ n.
\]
For some exponent patterns this model does admit opposite-side quadruples, so
no blanket mixed local-sterility theorem should be expected.

Thus the remaining lifted local obstruction is concentrated in the mixed
codimension-two case, together with possible infinite-order flatness and the
global recurrence / re-entry problem.
\end{remark}
```

This is the cleanest way to get the new structural lesson into the draft without overclaiming.

In the remark

```latex
\begin{remark}[Current state of the argument]
\label{rem:current-state-of-argument}
```

find the ending paragraph:

```latex
In parallel, the finite-core endgame is now locally reduced to two explicit
tasks: extracting the multi-pair interaction defects
$E_{\ge2}^{(3)},E_{\ge2}^{(5)},\overline E_{\ge2}^{(7)}$ in the corrected
finite-order model, and proving zero-capture for the lifted order-$7$
determinants $K_v$ and $T_v$ on $v_5\neq 0$ patches.
```

Replace it with:

```latex
In parallel, the finite-core endgame now splits into a global bridge and a
local lifted problem. On the global side, the two-pair algebra forces
quantitative near-coincidence of the strengthened invariant \(\widehat\Psi\),
and on good \(M\neq 0\) patches this forces bad two-pair cores into a
defect-scale close regime. On the local side, what remains is to extract the
multi-pair interaction defects
\[
E_{\ge2}^{(3)},\qquad E_{\ge2}^{(5)},\qquad \overline E_{\ge2}^{(7)},
\]
and to prove the required zero-capture / first-contact statements for the
lifted order-$7$ determinants \(K_v\) and \(T_v\) on \(v_5\neq 0\) patches,
with the mixed case \(K_v=T_v=0\) isolated as the genuine codimension-two
local obstruction.
```

_Thought for 33s_

Yes. This looks strong overall, and I think the route is now genuinely coherent: the quantitative \(\widehat\Psi\)-bridge handles the far-apart regime, and the lifted \(K_v/T_v\) geometry handles the close-point regime, with the mixed locus \(K_v=T_v=0\) isolated as the real local obstruction. That synthesis is the right one. 

What I would still **check carefully** before treating it as theorem-ready:

1. The septic defect norm and representative choice. The algebra with
\[
\det(R,A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f})
\]
looks right, but the theorem should explicitly define the quotient norm as
\[
|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|
:=
\inf_{R\mapsto \overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}} |R|,
\]
so the quantitative estimate is visibly canonical. The note already gestures at this; I would make it explicit in the paper. 

2. The exact bridge from a bad two-pair core to the local lifted configuration. The concentration theorem outline assumes that once \(h_1,h_2\) are close, the bad-core equations really produce the sort of local lifted affine-dependence pattern that the \(K_v/T_v\) sterility theorems exclude. I think that is true, but that is the place where a proof can accidentally hand-wave. The note itself flags this as the main thing still to formalize. 

3. The concentration theorem should probably be stated with a slightly stronger hypothesis than just “compact good patch.” I would include: \(c\neq0\), \(M\neq0\), \(v_5\neq0\), isolated finite-order zeros of \(K_v\), and isolated finite-order zeros of \(T_v\) on \(\{K_v\neq0\}\). Otherwise the theorem will sound more global than the current local sterility inputs justify. 

What I think I can **attack independently right now** is not the full concentration theorem, but the missing middle bridge:

**Local exclusion of close-point bad two-pair cores away from \(K_v=T_v=0\).**

That is the right intermediate theorem between the quantitative \(\widehat\Psi\)-estimate and the full concentration-to-mixed-locus statement. The note already identifies this as the sharp next target. turn6file8

Why that is the best next attack:

If you already know
\[
|h_1-h_2|\lesssim \varepsilon
\]
with \(\varepsilon\) the total defect size, then away from the mixed locus there are only three possibilities near an accumulation point \(h_*\):

- \(T_v(h_*)\neq 0\), so the lifted quadruple is locally ECT and the close-point bad-core pattern should be impossible.
- \(T_v(h_*)=0\) but \(K_v(h_*)\neq 0\), so the finite-order \(T_v\)-sterility theorem should kill local lifted 4-point birth.
- \(K_v(h_*)=0\) but \(T_v(h_*)\neq 0\), which is again locally sign-regular at the order-4 level.

So the only place left should be
\[
K_v=T_v=0.
\]
That is exactly the concentration-to-mixed-locus idea in the note, but the intermediate theorem is easier to write rigorously and does not overclaim. 

One small thing I would also check in the note: there is a sentence saying \(x=v_5/c\) is the “first coordinate” of \(\widehat\Psi\), but in the current convention it is the second. That is harmless, but worth cleaning before it gets copied into the paper. 

So my bottom line is:

The algebraic bridge looks good.
The patchwise defect-scale closeness corollary looks good.
The full concentration theorem is plausible but still wants one careful bridging lemma.
The cleanest thing to attack next, independently, is the **local exclusion away from the mixed locus**, because once that is written cleanly the concentration theorem becomes nearly formal. turn6file8

If you want, I can draft that intermediate theorem in exact proof-outline form against the current paper notation.
## User (2026-04-19 16:38:20)

OK do those checks and do the math for any needed intermediate proof. note one more update from the other chat FYI:

Here’s the cleanest way I see to continue the attack.

The first thing to say is that the picture has sharpened enough that we should now **separate the endgame by core size**. The two-point route and the lifted (3/4)-point route are related, but they are not the same obstruction.

For a **two-point** bad core, the new quantitative theorem for
[
\widehat\Psi(h)=\left(\frac{u_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)^2}\right)
]
is the main tool: it forces near-coincidence of (\widehat\Psi(h_1)) and (\widehat\Psi(h_2)). On a good patch with (c\neq 0) and (M\neq 0), the second coordinate
[
x(h)=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}
]
has derivative
[
x'(h)=\frac{M(h)}{c(h)^2},
]
so (x) is locally bi-Lipschitz. Thus
[
|h_1-h_2|
\lesssim
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+|E_{12}^{(5)}|+|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|.
]
That is already real progress. 

But the next move is to ask what **defect order** is sufficient to kill two-point bad cores outright.

If we can prove on good patches that the interaction defects are actually **superlinear** in the separation,
[
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+|E_{12}^{(5)}|+|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|
========================================================

o(|h_1-h_2|),
]
or even better
[
O(|h_1-h_2|^2),
]
then two-point bad cores are impossible on such patches.

The proof would be simple. Write
[
\widehat\Psi(h_2)-\widehat\Psi(h_1)
===================================

(h_2-h_1)\widehat\Psi'(h_1)+O(|h_2-h_1|^2).
]
Since the second coordinate derivative is
[
\left(\frac{v_5}{c}\right)'=\frac{M}{c^2}\neq 0,
]
we have (\widehat\Psi'(h_1)\neq 0). So if the right-hand side from the defect theorem were (o(|h_2-h_1|)), the only possibility would be (h_2=h_1). In other words:

> **Sufficient next theorem for the two-point branch:**
> prove the interaction defects are superlinear in pair separation on good patches.

That would kill the entire two-point bad-core regime away from patch boundaries. This is now the sharpest next algebraic target. 

Now for **three- and four-point minimal bad cores**, the correct local objects are the lifted determinants (K_v) and (T_v). The other chat’s summary makes the stratification very clear:

* finite-order isolated (K_v=0) is locally sterile for lifted (3)-point birth;
* finite-order isolated (T_v=0) with (K_v\neq 0) is locally sterile for lifted (4)-point birth;
* the mixed codimension-two locus
  [
  K_v=T_v=0
  ]
  is the one local case not killed by the blanket sterility mechanism, because its monomial normal form can have a generalized-Vandermonde vanishing. 

So the continuation there is:

> **Local exclusion away from the mixed locus:**
> prove that on a (v_5\neq 0) patch, close-point bad (3)-point or (4)-point affine circuits cannot accumulate at a point where either (K_v\neq 0), or (T_v\neq 0), or (T_v=0) but (K_v\neq 0) with finite order.

That would immediately imply a concentration theorem:

> any small-defect bad finite core on a good patch can accumulate only at the mixed lifted locus (K_v=T_v=0), or at patch boundaries / turning boundaries.

So the attack sequence now looks like this:

First, finish the **two-point algebraic branch** by proving a superlinear defect estimate. That is the cleanest next theorem.

Second, formalize the **local close-point exclusion** for the lifted (3/4)-point branches away from (K_v=T_v=0). This uses the sterility theorems the other chat outlined. 

Third, conclude the concentration theorem:
bad finite cores with small defects can only survive near the mixed lifted locus (or other non-good boundaries).

So the route has become much sharper:

* **two-point cores** are now mainly an **interaction-defect order** problem;
* **three/four-point cores** are now mainly a **lifted mixed-locus** problem.

That is a much better split than before.

My next attack angle, concretely, would be:

### Prove a superlinear defect estimate for the two-point branch

Try to show that on a good compact patch,
[
E_{12}^{(3)},\ E_{12}^{(5)},\ \overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}
]
all vanish at least quadratically in (|h_1-h_2|) after the normalized core reduction. If that is true, the two-point bad-core branch dies immediately.

That is the cleanest next mathematical target I see.

_Thought for 3m 42s_

I checked the logic, and I think the picture is now sharper than it was even a few turns ago.

The first thing I would say plainly is this:

The new quantitative \(\widehat\Psi\)-bridge is a real theorem target. The algebra for the first two coordinates is straightforward, and the third-coordinate formula using a representative \(R\) of the septic quotient defect is the right packaging; \(\det(R,A_{5,1}^{\mathfrak f})\) is indeed representative-independent. So that part looks mathematically sound.

The second thing is a correction to the current roadmap:

For the **two-point branch**, the lifted \(K_v/T_v\) sterility theorems are not yet enough by themselves. They control local birth of lifted 3-point and 4-point affine configurations, but a bad **two-point** core only gives near-coincidence of \(\widehat\Psi(h_1)\) and \(\widehat\Psi(h_2)\). That is not automatically the same thing as a 3-point or 4-point lifted incidence configuration. So the “concentration to \(K_v=T_v=0\)” theorem is not yet proved even heuristically until that missing bridge is written carefully.

That means the other chat’s newest split by core size is exactly the right one:

For **two-point cores**, the next clean target is a superlinear defect estimate.

For **three- and four-point minimal cores**, the next clean target is the lifted mixed-locus analysis.

Here is the intermediate math that is already solid.

Assume you have the quantitative theorem in the form
\[
\bigl|\widehat\Psi(h_1)-\widehat\Psi(h_2)\bigr|
\le C\,\varepsilon,
\qquad
\varepsilon:=|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|
\]
on a compact good patch, where
\[
\widehat\Psi(h)=\left(\frac{u_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)^2}\right).
\]

On a compact interval \(J\Subset\{c\neq0,\ M\neq0\}\), the second coordinate
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}
\]
satisfies
\[
x'(h)=\frac{M(h)}{c(h)^2},
\]
so \(x\) is bi-Lipschitz on \(J\). Hence there is a constant \(\eta_J>0\) such that
\[
|x(h_1)-x(h_2)|\ge \eta_J |h_1-h_2|.
\]
Since \(x\) is a coordinate of \(\widehat\Psi\),
\[
|\widehat\Psi(h_1)-\widehat\Psi(h_2)|\ge \eta_J |h_1-h_2|.
\]
Combining the two gives
\[
|h_1-h_2|\le C_J\,\varepsilon.
\]

So the defect-scale closeness corollary is correct.

Now here is the useful intermediate theorem that follows immediately and is worth isolating.

Let
\[
\delta:=|h_1-h_2|.
\]
If on a good patch the defects are actually **sublinear** in the separation,
\[
\varepsilon=o(\delta)
\qquad(\delta\to 0),
\]
then bad two-point cores are impossible.

Proof: from the quantitative theorem and the lower Lipschitz bound,
\[
\eta_J \delta
\le |\widehat\Psi(h_1)-\widehat\Psi(h_2)|
\le C\,\varepsilon.
\]
If \(\varepsilon=o(\delta)\), this becomes
\[
\eta_J \delta \le o(\delta),
\]
which is impossible for \(\delta>0\) small.

So the really sharp sufficient theorem for the two-point branch is:

> If the interaction defects are \(o(|h_1-h_2|)\) on good patches, then the two-point bad-core branch is dead there.

And of course the stronger estimate
\[
\varepsilon=O(\delta^2)
\]
would kill it immediately as well.

That is the cleanest intermediate proof I can give right now.

What does this mean practically?

It means the next two-point target is not “use \(K_v,T_v\) somehow.” It is:

> prove a superlinear defect estimate for
> \[
> E_{12}^{(3)},\quad E_{12}^{(5)},\quad \overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}
> \]
> on compact good patches.

That is now the sharpest algebraic target for the two-point regime.

There is also one promising structural route to that estimate.

If the raw interaction defects, viewed in midpoint/separation variables
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad s=h_2-h_1,
\]
satisfy both:
\[
E^{(j)}(m,0)=0
\]
and
\[
E^{(j)}(m,s)=E^{(j)}(m,-s)
\]
for \(j=3,5,7\),
then automatically
\[
E^{(j)}(m,s)=O(s^2).
\]
So a quadratic defect estimate would follow from two things:
vanishing on the diagonal, and evenness in the separation variable.

I do **not** think the draft currently proves either of those for the corrected interaction defects. But that is now a very concrete algebraic check to make. If the corrected two-pair interaction is genuinely symmetric under swapping the two pair inputs and collapses on the diagonal, then \(O(\delta^2)\) may come almost for free.

That is the main independent attack I see.

On the lifted side, here is what I think is true.

For three- and four-point minimal bad cores, the local picture really is stratified:

finite-order isolated \(K_v=0\) is locally sterile for lifted 3-point birth;

finite-order isolated \(T_v=0\) with \(K_v\neq 0\) is locally sterile for lifted 4-point birth;

the mixed case
\[
K_v=T_v=0
\]
is genuinely different and cannot be killed by the same blanket finite-order sterility theorem, because the monomial normal form
\[
Q_v(x)\sim (x^n,x,x^m)
\]
can have generalized-Vandermonde vanishing.

So for three/four-point branches, the remaining real local target is the mixed locus.

But for the two-point branch, the immediate next proof is still the superlinear defect theorem.

So my clean verdict is:

The checks pass on the quantitative \(\widehat\Psi\) theorem and the defect-scale closeness corollary.

The concentration-to-mixed-locus theorem is not yet ready; its missing bridge is real.

The best independent thing to attack now is the two-point branch via superlinear defect estimates.

And the exact intermediate theorem already proved is:

> On a compact good patch, if the total two-pair defect is \(o(|h_1-h_2|)\), then no nontrivial bad two-point core can exist there.

That is the one result I would promote immediately while the rest is being developed.

_Thought for 38s_

Yes. This is ready to insert now, and it should go **immediately after** the proof of

```latex
\begin{corollary}[Bad two-pair cores are defect-scale close on good $M\neq 0$ patches]
\label{cor:bad-two-pair-cores-defect-scale-close}
...
\end{corollary}
```

and **immediately before**

```latex
\begin{remark}[What remains in the two-pair rewrite]
\label{rem:what-remains-two-pair-rewrite}
```

Insert this block:

```latex
\begin{proposition}[No sublinear two-pair defects on good patches]
\label{prop:no-sublinear-two-pair-defects-good-patch}
Let
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}
\]
be a compact interval. Then there exists a constant \(C_J>0\) such that every
normalized bad two-pair core with \(h_1,h_2\in J\) and \(h_1\neq h_2\)
satisfies
\[
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+\bigl|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}\bigr|
\ge
C_J^{-1}\,|h_1-h_2|.
\]
In particular, there is no sequence of nontrivial normalized bad two-pair
cores in \(J\) for which
\[
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+\bigl|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}\bigr|
=
o\!\left(|h_1-h_2|\right).
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By Corollary~\ref{cor:bad-two-pair-cores-defect-scale-close}, there exists a
constant \(C'_J>0\) such that
\[
|h_1-h_2|
\le
C'_J\Bigl(|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|\Bigr)
\]
for every normalized bad two-pair core with \(h_1,h_2\in J\). Since
\[
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|
\le
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+\bigl|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}\bigr|,
\]
the same estimate yields
\[
|h_1-h_2|
\le
C'_J\Bigl(
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+\bigl|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}\bigr|
\Bigr).
\]
Renaming \(C'_J\) as \(C_J\) proves the first claim. The final statement is
immediate: if a sequence satisfied
\[
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+\bigl|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}\bigr|
=
o\!\left(|h_1-h_2|\right),
\]
then the displayed lower bound would force
\[
|h_1-h_2|=o\!\left(|h_1-h_2|\right),
\]
which is impossible for a nontrivial two-point core with \(h_1\neq h_2\).
\end{proof}
```

A very small optional follow-up edit: in the remark right after it, you could change

```latex
bad two-pair cores are forced into a defect-scale close regime
```

to

```latex
bad two-pair cores are forced into a defect-scale close regime, and
Proposition~\ref{prop:no-sublinear-two-pair-defects-good-patch} shows that the
two-point branch is already excluded whenever the total defect is sublinear in
the pair separation
```

If you want that exact replacement sentence too, use:

```latex
Theorem~\ref{thm:quantitative-defect-thickened-strengthened-coincidence}
supplies the correct algebraic bridge from the two-pair core to the
strengthened invariant \(\widehat\Psi\). In particular, on good \(M\neq 0\)
patches, bad two-pair cores are forced into a defect-scale close regime by
Corollary~\ref{cor:bad-two-pair-cores-defect-scale-close}, and
Proposition~\ref{prop:no-sublinear-two-pair-defects-good-patch} shows that the
two-point branch is already excluded whenever the total defect is sublinear in
the pair separation.
```

_Thought for 1m 12s_

Yes. There are two places I can jump in usefully right now.

The first is a **real theorem I’m comfortable certifying independently**: the shrinking-core blow-up lemmas on a good \(v_5\)-patch. The other chat’s formulation is right. If
\[
\Delta_3(x_1,x_2,x_3)=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1&x_1&S(x_1)\\
1&x_2&S(x_2)\\
1&x_3&S(x_3)
\end{pmatrix}=0
\]
for distinct nodes \(x_i\to x_*\), then after factoring out the Vandermonde, the remaining second divided difference tends to \(\tfrac12 S''(x_*)\), so shrinking exact lifted 3-point circuits can only accumulate at \(K_v=0\). Likewise, for the 4-point coplanarity determinant
\[
\Delta_4(x_1,x_2,x_3,x_4)=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1&Y(x_1)&x_1&S(x_1)\\
1&Y(x_2)&x_2&S(x_2)\\
1&Y(x_3)&x_3&S(x_3)\\
1&Y(x_4)&x_4&S(x_4)
\end{pmatrix}=0,
\]
the generalized divided-difference factorization shows the quotient tends to a constant multiple of
\[
W_x(1,Y,x,S)=T_v(x_*),
\]
so shrinking exact lifted 4-point circuits can only accumulate at \(T_v=0\). That part is mathematically clean and I think you can treat it as theorem-ready. 

The second is a **check/correction** on the broader concentration theorem. I do **not** think the full statement
“small-defect bad two-pair cores concentrate only at \(K_v=T_v=0\)”
is ready yet. The other chat already identifies the missing bridge correctly: you still need an exact theorem translating a bad two-pair core with \(h_1\approx h_2\) into the specific lifted affine-dependence pattern that the \(K_v/T_v\) sterility theorems exclude. Right now, the shrinking-core lemmas apply to exact lifted 3-point and 4-point circuits, while a bad two-point core only gives near-coincidence of \(\widehat\Psi(h_1)\) and \(\widehat\Psi(h_2)\). Those are related, but not yet the same theorem. So I would not overclaim that last concentration statement until that bridge is written. turn11file4turn11file6

What I think I can help with independently is this:

The sharpest next target for the **two-point branch** is the superlinear-defect theorem the other chat mentions. If on a good compact patch you can prove
\[
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|
=o(|h_1-h_2|),
\]
or better \(O(|h_1-h_2|^2)\), then the two-point branch dies immediately by the quantitative \(\widehat\Psi\)-coincidence estimate and the bi-Lipschitz lower bound from \(x=v_5/c\). That really is the cleanest algebraic target for the 2-point case. turn11file11

There is one concrete way to attack that superlinear estimate which neither chat has fully exploited yet:

Write the two-pair interaction defects in midpoint/separation variables
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad s=h_2-h_1.
\]
If you can show for the corrected interaction terms that

\[
E^{(j)}(m,0)=0
\]
and
\[
E^{(j)}(m,s)=E^{(j)}(m,-s)
\]
for \(j=3,5,7\),

then automatically
\[
E^{(j)}(m,s)=O(s^2).
\]

That would give the quadratic defect estimate you want essentially for free. So the concrete algebraic question I’d attack next is:

> are the corrected two-pair interaction defects diagonal-vanishing and even in the separation variable?

If yes, the two-point branch is probably finished away from bad boundaries.

On the **3/4-point lifted side**, the best thing to formalize next is the weaker but rigorous statement:

> On a compact good \(v_5\)-patch, shrinking exact lifted 3-point circuits can only accumulate at \(K_v=0\), and shrinking exact lifted 4-point circuits can only accumulate at \(T_v=0\); under the finite-order sterility theorems, the only surviving shrinking 4-point obstruction is the mixed locus \(K_v=T_v=0\), while shrinking 3-point obstructions require infinite-order flatness of \(K_v\). turn11file5turn11file8

That theorem is stronger than a roadmap and weaker than the full bad-core concentration theorem, which is exactly why I think it is the right next formal step.

So my bottom line is:

The shrinking-core blow-up lemmas are solid.

The full concentration-to-\(K_v=T_v=0\) theorem is still one bridge short.

The best independent thing to attack now is the **superlinear defect estimate** for two-point cores, most likely via midpoint/separation symmetry of the corrected interaction defects.

And the best immediate theorem you can safely add on the lifted side is the **shrinking exact lifted circuit concentration** statement.

## Assistant (2026-04-19 16:56:52)

_Thought for 4m 17s_

Yes. The clean bridge you can add mathematically, right now, is a **repeated-node/tangent-contact bridge**. It does not finish the full concentration theorem, but it gives the exact missing local translation from “two nearby support points” to the lifted \(K_v/T_v\) geometry.

Here is the right setup on a good \(v_5\)-patch:
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c},\qquad
S=\sigma_v=\frac{\Delta_7}{cv_5},
\]
so
\[
\Lambda_v=(x,S),\qquad
Q_v=(Y,x,S).
\]

Because \(M\neq 0\), \(x\) is a valid local coordinate.

## 1. The exact repeated-node bridge for the 3-point lifted branch

Define the collinearity determinant
\[
\Delta_3(a,b,u):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & a & S(a)\\
1 & b & S(b)\\
1 & u & S(u)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Then
\[
\frac{\Delta_3(a,b,u)}{b-a}
\longrightarrow
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & a & S(a)\\
0 & 1 & S'(a)\\
1 & u & S(u)
\end{pmatrix}
\qquad (b\to a).
\]

That is just differentiating the second row with respect to \(b\). The limiting determinant says exactly:

> if a secant through \((a,S(a))\) and \((b,S(b))\) persists as \(b\to a\), then the limit point \((u,S(u))\) lies on the **tangent line** to \(\Lambda_v\) at \(a\).

So the rigorous bridge is:

- a shrinking exact lifted 3-point circuit does **not** just “go near \(K_v=0\)”;
- if only one pair collapses, it limits to a **tangent-line contact** configuration for \(\Lambda_v\);
- if all three points collapse, then the divided-difference limit gives \(K_v(a)=0\).

So there are really two close-point regimes for 3-point circuits:
tangent-line contact with one repeated node, and full triple collapse to \(K_v=0\).

## 2. The exact repeated-node bridge for the 4-point lifted branch

Define the coplanarity determinant
\[
\Delta_4(a,b,u,v):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & Y(a) & a & S(a)\\
1 & Y(b) & b & S(b)\\
1 & Y(u) & u & S(u)\\
1 & Y(v) & v & S(v)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Then
\[
\frac{\Delta_4(a,b,u,v)}{b-a}
\longrightarrow
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & Y(a) & a & S(a)\\
0 & Y'(a) & 1 & S'(a)\\
1 & Y(u) & u & S(u)\\
1 & Y(v) & v & S(v)
\end{pmatrix}
\qquad (b\to a).
\]

Again this is just the row-derivative limit. The limiting determinant says:

> if a coplanar quadruple has one pair collapsing, then the two surviving points and the collapsing point lie in the **tangent plane** to \(Q_v\) at the collision point.

So for the 4-point branch the exact bridge is:

- one collapsing pair gives a **tangent-plane contact** configuration for \(Q_v\);
- full quadruple collapse gives \(T_v(a)=0\) by the divided-difference/Wronskian limit.

## 3. Why this is the right missing bridge

This is exactly what was missing in the informal concentration-to-\(K_v=T_v=0\) claim.

The sterility theorems you already have are about local birth of collinear/coplanar configurations across \(K_v\)- or \(T_v\)-boundaries.

What was not yet written was the passage from a close support pair to those local geometric objects.

These two determinant limits give that passage exactly:

- nearby support points produce **tangent-line** or **tangent-plane** contact in the lifted geometry;
- total collapse produces \(K_v=0\) or \(T_v=0\).

So the real local obstruction away from the mixed locus is not just “close points.” It is:
- tangent-line contact for \(\Lambda_v\),
- tangent-plane contact for \(Q_v\),
- or full collapse to \(K_v=0\) or \(T_v=0\).

## 4. What this proves immediately

It proves a clean intermediate theorem for **exact lifted circuits**:

On a good \(v_5\)-patch, any shrinking sequence of exact lifted 3-point circuits has a subsequence converging either to:
- a tangent-line contact configuration for \(\Lambda_v\), or
- a full collapse point with \(K_v=0\).

Likewise, any shrinking sequence of exact lifted 4-point circuits has a subsequence converging either to:
- a tangent-plane contact configuration for \(Q_v\), or
- a full collapse point with \(T_v=0\).

That is rigorous now.

## 5. What still remains for the full concentration theorem

The remaining gap is now much narrower:

You still need to show that, on a good patch away from the mixed locus and away from any explicit exceptional coincidence set, these tangent-line/tangent-plane contact configurations cannot persist.

So the full concentration theorem is not yet:
\[
\text{close bad cores} \Longrightarrow K_v=T_v=0.
\]

What you do have is:
\[
\text{close bad cores}
\Longrightarrow
\text{tangent contact or full collapse in the lifted geometry}.
\]

That is the right bridge.

## 6. How I would split the next math

There are now two separate next tasks.

For the **two-point branch**, the clean next target is still the superlinear defect estimate:
\[
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|
=o(|h_1-h_2|),
\]
or ideally \(O(|h_1-h_2|^2)\). If that holds, the two-point branch dies immediately on good patches.

For the **3/4-point branch**, the clean next target is:
prove that on a good \(v_5\)-patch away from the mixed locus, the tangent-line and tangent-plane contact limits above are locally sterile.

That is now a sharply formulated local theorem, not a vague bridge.

So yes — this is the bridge I can add right now: collapsing pairs become **repeated-node tangent-contact determinants** in the lifted geometry. That is the exact local translation that was missing.

## Assistant (2026-04-19 17:00:53)

_Thought for 2m 43s_

Yes. The clean next bridge I can prove is this:

**shrinking close-point lifted configurations are forced into repeated-node tangent-contact configurations, and away from the zero sets of \(K_v\) and \(T_v\) those repeated-node configurations are uniformly excluded.**

That is a real intermediate theorem. It does not yet finish the full bad-core concentration theorem, but it supplies the exact local bridge that was missing.

Work on a compact good \(v_5\)-patch, and use
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c},\qquad
S=\sigma_v=\frac{\Delta_7}{cv_5},
\]
so
\[
\Lambda_v=(x,S),\qquad
Q_v=(Y,x,S).
\]
Since \(M\neq 0\), \(x\) is a valid local coordinate.

Now define the repeated-node contact determinants.

For the planar lifted curve \(\Lambda_v\), set
\[
H_3(a,u):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & a & S(a)\\
0 & 1 & S'(a)\\
1 & u & S(u)
\end{pmatrix}
=
S(u)-S(a)-S'(a)(u-a).
\]
This vanishes exactly when the point \((u,S(u))\) lies on the tangent line to \(\Lambda_v\) at \(a\).

For the spatial lifted curve \(Q_v\), set
\[
H_4(a,u,v):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & Y(a) & a & S(a)\\
0 & Y'(a) & 1 & S'(a)\\
1 & Y(u) & u & S(u)\\
1 & Y(v) & v & S(v)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
This vanishes exactly when the two points \(Q_v(u),Q_v(v)\) lie in the tangent plane to \(Q_v\) at \(a\).

These are the correct repeated-node limits of the exact 3-point and 4-point determinants.

Indeed, if
\[
\Delta_3(a,b,u):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & a & S(a)\\
1 & b & S(b)\\
1 & u & S(u)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
then
\[
\frac{\Delta_3(a,b,u)}{b-a}\longrightarrow H_3(a,u)
\qquad (b\to a).
\]

Likewise, if
\[
\Delta_4(a,b,u,v):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & Y(a) & a & S(a)\\
1 & Y(b) & b & S(b)\\
1 & Y(u) & u & S(u)\\
1 & Y(v) & v & S(v)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
then
\[
\frac{\Delta_4(a,b,u,v)}{b-a}\longrightarrow H_4(a,u,v)
\qquad (b\to a).
\]

So the exact local bridge is:

- a collapsing pair in a 3-point lifted circuit limits to a **tangent-line contact** \(H_3=0\),
- a collapsing pair in a 4-point lifted circuit limits to a **tangent-plane contact** \(H_4=0\).

Now here is the key local attack.

## 1. Uniform exclusion of tangent-line contact away from \(K_v=0\)

Expand \(H_3\) at \(u=a+r\):
\[
H_3(a,a+r)
=
S(a+r)-S(a)-S'(a)r
=
\frac12 S''(a)r^2+\frac16 S'''(a)r^3+O(r^4).
\]

Since \(K_v\) is exactly the curvature numerator of \(\Lambda_v\), in \(x\)-coordinates it is \(S''\) up to a nowhere-zero factor. So on any compact set where \(|K_v|\ge \kappa>0\), we have \(|S''|\ge c_\kappa>0\). Therefore there exists \(\varepsilon>0\) such that for all \(a\) in that compact set,
\[
0<|u-a|<\varepsilon
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
H_3(a,u)\neq 0.
\]

So tangent-line contact is uniformly excluded near the diagonal away from \(K_v=0\).

This immediately reproves the shrinking 3-point concentration statement in a stronger form:
any shrinking exact lifted 3-point circuit either accumulates at \(K_v=0\), or else it is impossible.

## 2. Uniform exclusion of tangent-plane contact away from \(T_v=0\)

Now expand \(H_4\) for \(u=a+r\), \(v=a+s\).

Subtract from the \(u\)-row the combination
\[
\text{row}_1+r\,\text{row}_2,
\]
and from the \(v\)-row the combination
\[
\text{row}_1+s\,\text{row}_2.
\]
Then the transformed lower rows become
\[
\bigl(0,\ \tfrac12 Y''(a)r^2+\tfrac16 Y'''(a)r^3+O(r^4),\ 0,\ \tfrac12 S''(a)r^2+\tfrac16 S'''(a)r^3+O(r^4)\bigr),
\]
and similarly with \(r\) replaced by \(s\).

So the determinant reduces to the \(2\times2\) determinant in the \(Y,S\) entries, and the leading term is
\[
H_4(a,a+r,a+s)
=
\frac{1}{12}\bigl(Y''(a)S'''(a)-Y'''(a)S''(a)\bigr)\,r^2s^2(s-r)
+O(\rho^6),
\]
where
\[
\rho:=\max\{|r|,|s|\}.
\]

But
\[
Y''S'''-Y'''S''=W_x(1,Y,x,S)=T_v
\]
in the \(x\)-coordinate. Therefore
\[
H_4(a,a+r,a+s)
=
\frac{T_v(a)}{12}\,r^2s^2(s-r)+O(\rho^6).
\]

So on any compact set where \(|T_v|\ge \tau>0\), there exists \(\varepsilon>0\) such that whenever
\[
0<|u-a|,\ |v-a|,\ |u-v|<\varepsilon,
\]
we have
\[
H_4(a,u,v)\neq 0.
\]

Thus tangent-plane contact is uniformly excluded near the diagonal away from \(T_v=0\).

This gives the precise local bridge that was missing.

## 3. What this proves now

It proves a genuine intermediate theorem:

On a compact good \(v_5\)-patch, any shrinking exact lifted 3-point circuit has accumulation points only in \(K_v=0\), and any shrinking exact lifted 4-point circuit has accumulation points only in \(T_v=0\).

More sharply:

- away from \(K_v=0\), there is a uniform diagonal collar with no tangent-line contacts and hence no shrinking 3-point circuits;
- away from \(T_v=0\), there is a uniform diagonal collar with no tangent-plane contacts and hence no shrinking 4-point circuits with one collapsing pair.

And combining this with the finite-order sterility theorems gives:

- shrinking 3-point lifted obstructions can only persist at **infinite-order flatness of \(K_v\)**;
- shrinking 4-point lifted obstructions can only persist at **infinite-order flatness of \(T_v\)** or at the mixed locus
  \[
  K_v=T_v=0.
  \]

That is now a real theorem-level reduction.

## 4. What this still does not do

It still does **not** finish the full bad-two-pair concentration theorem.

The remaining gap is that a bad two-point core gives near-coincidence of \(\widehat\Psi(h_1)\) and \(\widehat\Psi(h_2)\), but not yet an exact lifted 3-point or 4-point circuit. So one still needs a separate argument to turn a collapsing pair in a bad core into one of these repeated-node tangent-contact patterns, or else kill the two-point branch directly by a superlinear defect estimate.

So the clean split is now:

For the **two-point branch**, the next exact target remains:
prove
\[
|E_{12}^{(3)}|+\|E_{12}^{(5)}\|+|\overline E_{12}^{(7;1)}|
=o(|h_1-h_2|),
\]
or better \(O(|h_1-h_2|^2)\), on good patches.

For the **3/4-point lifted branch**, the exact local bridge is now done:
shrinking configurations limit to tangent-line or tangent-plane contacts, and those are uniformly excluded away from \(K_v=0\) and \(T_v=0\).

So this is the bridge I can add now, mathematically:
**close lifted configurations become repeated-node tangent-contact problems, and those are uniformly impossible away from the \(K_v\)- and \(T_v\)-zero sets.**
