_Thought for 1m 39s_

I attacked it as hard as I can from the current draft, and here is the strongest clean outcome.

## 1. The real invariant is \(\mathcal Z\), and the dream identity is about \(\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'\)

On a good \(v_5\)-patch, write
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}.
\]
Then the exact lifted \(4\)-point identities are
\[
K_v=(x')^3S''(x),
\]
and
\[
T_v=(x')^6\bigl(S''Y'''-S'''Y''\bigr).
\]

So on every nonmixed patch \(K_v\neq 0\), the canonical scalar is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''},
\qquad
\mathcal R:=\mathcal Z'
=
\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}.
}
\]

This is better than \(Y\) itself, because \(\mathcal Z\) and \(\mathcal R\) are invariant under the affine gauge
\[
Y\mapsto Y+\alpha+\beta x+\gamma S
\]
that preserves coplanarity: indeed
\[
Y''\mapsto Y''+\gamma S'',
\qquad
\mathcal Z\mapsto \mathcal Z+\gamma,
\qquad
\mathcal R\mapsto \mathcal R.
\]

So the 4-point branch is really about \(\mathcal R\), not about \(Y\) in an arbitrary chart.

---

## 2. Exact nonmixed reduction

At a nonmixed point
\[
K_v(0)\neq 0,\qquad T_v(0)=0,
\]
the analytic Morse lemma lets you normalize to
\[
Q_v(\xi)=\bigl(\widetilde Y(\xi),\,\xi,\,\xi^2\bigr),
\]
with
\[
\widetilde Y(0)=\widetilde Y'(0)=\widetilde Y''(0)=0.
\]

Then
\[
S''=2,
\qquad
\mathcal Z(\xi)=\frac12\,\widetilde Y''(\xi),
\qquad
\mathcal R(\xi)=\frac12\,\widetilde Y'''(\xi).
\]

So the dangerous nonmixed low-order cases
\[
n=4,\ 6,\ 8
\]
are exactly
\[
\ord_0\mathcal Z=2,\ 4,\ 6
\qquad\text{equivalently}\qquad
\ord_0\mathcal R=1,\ 3,\ 5.
\]

That is the sharpest exact nonmixed reduction.

---

## 3. Exact mixed reduction

At a mixed point
\[
K_v(0)=0,\qquad T_v(0)=0,
\]
let
\[
r:=\ord_0 K_v,\qquad s:=\ord_0 T_v.
\]
Then
\[
S''(x)=\kappa x^r+O(x^{r+1}),
\qquad \kappa\neq 0,
\]
so after integrating twice,
\[
S(x)=\frac{\kappa}{(r+1)(r+2)}x^{r+2}+O(x^{r+3}),
\]
hence the mixed exponent is
\[
m=r+2.
\]

If the first term of \(Y\) not absorbed by \(\operatorname{span}\{1,x,S\}\) is
\[
Y(x)=\alpha x^n+O(x^{n+1}),
\qquad
\alpha\neq 0,\qquad n>r+2,
\]
then substituting into
\[
T_v=(x')^6\bigl(S''Y'''-S'''Y''\bigr)
\]
gives
\[
\ord_0 T_v=n+r-3,
\]
so
\[
n=s-r+3.
\]

Therefore
\[
\ord_0\mathcal Z
=
\ord_0\!\left(\frac{Y''}{S''}\right)
=
n-r-2
=
s-2r+1,
\]
and
\[
\ord_0\mathcal R=s-2r.
\]

So the dangerous mixed low-order list
\[
(m,n)=(3,4),\ (3,6),\ (4,6)
\]
is exactly
\[
(r,\ord_0\mathcal Z)=(1,1),\ (1,3),\ (2,2),
\]
equivalently
\[
(r,\ord_0\mathcal R)=(1,0),\ (1,2),\ (2,1).
\]

That is the sharpest exact mixed reduction.

---

## 4. The dream identity, correctly reformulated

The dream identity should **not** be phrased as “\(Y\) is odd” in an arbitrary chart.

The correct nonmixed formulation is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R(\xi)=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}
\text{ is even in the normalized local coordinate } \xi.
}
\]

Equivalently,
\[
\mathcal Z(\xi)=\frac{Y''}{S''}
\text{ is odd},
\]
and in the normalized chart \(S=\xi^2\),
\[
\widetilde Y(-\xi)=-\widetilde Y(\xi).
\]

This one identity would immediately kill the dangerous nonmixed cases, because an even \(\mathcal R\) cannot have odd-order zeros at \(0\).

There is also a mixed dream identity:

\[
\boxed{
\ord_0\mathcal Z\ge 4
\quad\text{at every mixed point.}
}
\]

That would kill the entire explicit low-order mixed list
\[
(m,n)=(3,4),\ (3,6),\ (4,6).
\]

So these are now the two exact theorem targets:

- nonmixed dream identity: \(\mathcal R\) even;
- mixed dream identity: \(\ord_0\mathcal Z\ge 4\).

---

## 5. What I found when hunting for such identities

Here is the critical negative theorem.

### Realization lemma
Every analytic pair of germs \((\widetilde Y,\widetilde S)\) occurs, after affine normalization, as the lifted pair
\[
\left(\frac{u_5}{c},\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}\right)
\]
on some good \(v_5\)-patch.

Indeed, take
\[
c(\xi)\equiv 1,
\qquad
v_5(\xi)=1+\xi,
\qquad
u_5(\xi)=\widetilde Y(\xi),
\qquad
\Delta_7(\xi)=(1+\xi)\widetilde S(\xi).
\]
Then
\[
c\neq 0,\qquad
v_5\neq 0,\qquad
M=c\,v_5'-c'v_5=1\neq 0,
\]
and after shifting \(x=v_5/c=1+\xi\) by \(1\),
\[
Y=\widetilde Y,\qquad S=\widetilde S.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No identity involving only }c,\ v_5,\ u_5,\ \Delta_7
\text{ and their derivatives can force the dream identity.}
}
\]

That is the strongest negative result from the hunt.

It means any true RH-specific obstruction must come from **deeper structure** not yet encoded in the lifted variables alone.

---

## 6. Where the missing identity could still live

There are only two plausible places left.

### A. Source-level involution symmetry

If there is a source-level corrected quotient-germ map with a local reflection symmetry near a one-pair source, and after nonmixed normalization that symmetry becomes
\[
\xi\mapsto -\xi,
\]
then the dream identity would follow:
\[
\widetilde Y(-\xi)=-\widetilde Y(\xi),
\qquad
\mathcal R(\xi)\ \text{even}.
\]

This is the most conceptual route.

### B. Matrix-level fixed-sector identity

The only other place a real RH-specific relation could live is in the deeper matrix construction behind \(u_5\) and \(\Delta_7\).

So one wants an identity that, after passage to the normalized chart, forces either

\[
\mathcal R \text{ even},
\]
or at least
\[
\ord_0\mathcal Z\ge 4
\]
at mixed points.

But I do **not** see any such identity currently latent in the paper.

---

The 4-point endgame is now reduced to the invariant pair
\[
(\mathcal Z,\mathcal R)
=
\left(\frac{Y''}{S''},\ \frac{d}{dx}\frac{Y''}{S''}\right).
\]

The low-order dangerous patterns are exactly:

### Nonmixed
\[
\ord_0\mathcal R\in\{1,3,5\}.

### Mixed
\[

\]

And the dream identities that would kill them are:

### Nonmixed dream identity
\[

\]

### Mixed dream identity

That is the sharpest formulation I can justify.

I did **not** find the RH-specific identity itself.

What I *did* find is:

- the exact mixed/nonmixed order bookkeeping in terms of \((\mathcal Z,\mathcal R)\),
- the correct reformulation of the dream identity,
- and a realization lemma proving that the identity cannot come from the current lifted variables alone.

So the hunt result is:

\boxed{

That is where the dream identity could still exist.

_Thought for 5m 13s_

Here is the strongest attack result I can justify now.

## 1. A new exact identity below the lifted variables

On a good \(v_5\)-patch, define
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5},
\qquad
\mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''},
\qquad
\mathcal R:=\mathcal Z'=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}.
\]
The exact identities
\[
K_v=(x')^3S'',
\qquad
T_v=(x')^6\bigl(S''Y'''-S'''Y''\bigr),
\qquad
\mathcal R=\frac{d}{dx}\!\left(\frac{Y''}{S''}\right)
\]
are already implicit in the current file and were correctly isolated in the other chat’s note. 

What is **new** is that \(\mathcal Z\) can be written as a ratio of two triple Wronskians built directly from the original scalar data \(c,v_5,u_5,\Delta_7\).

For any scalar germ \(f\), define
\[
J_f(h):=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
c(h) & v_5(h) & f(h)\\
c'(h) & v_5'(h) & f'(h)\\
c''(h) & v_5''(h) & f''(h)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Then a direct calculation gives the general formula
\[
\frac{d^2}{dx^2}\!\left(\frac{f}{c}\right)
=
\frac{c^3}{M^3}\,J_f,
\qquad
M=c\,v_5'-c'v_5.
\]

Applying this to \(f=u_5\) gives
\[
Y''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_{u_5}.
\]

Now set
\[
\lambda:=\frac{\Delta_7}{v_5}.
\]
Since
\[
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}=\frac{\lambda}{c},
\]
the same formula with \(f=\lambda\) gives
\[
S''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_{\lambda}.
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z=\frac{Y''}{S''}=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}.
}
\]

And since
\[
\mathcal R=\frac{d}{dx}\mathcal Z
=
\frac{c^2}{M}\frac{d}{dh}\!\left(\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}\right),
\]
we also get
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R=\frac{c^2}{M}\frac{d}{dh}\!\left(\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}\right).
}
\]

That is the cleanest new identity I found.

It says the entire 4-point branch is governed by the ratio of two triple Wronskians:
one from \((c,v_5,u_5)\), the other from \((c,v_5,\lambda)\) with \(\lambda=\Delta_7/v_5\).

So any RH-specific obstruction has to show up as a structural relation between
\[
J_{u_5}
\quad\text{and}\quad
J_{\lambda}.
\]

## 2. What this means for the dream identity

The dream identity was:

- nonmixed: \(\mathcal R\) is even in the normalized local coordinate;
- mixed: \(\ord_0 \mathcal Z\ge 4\).

In terms of the new Wronskian ratio, these become:

### Nonmixed dream identity
After nonmixed normalization,
\[
\boxed{
\frac{d}{d\xi}\!\left(\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}\right)
\text{ is even in the normalized local coordinate } \xi.
}
\]

### Mixed dream identity
At a mixed point,
\[
\boxed{
\ord_0\!\left(\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}\right)\ge 4.
}
\]

So the dream identity is no longer a statement about \(Y\) directly. It is a statement about the **ratio of the two triple Wronskians**.

That is a cleaner target, because it sits one layer below the lifted variables but still uses only quantities already present in the file.

## 3. Why this still does not finish the job

This identity is useful, but it does **not** yet give the RH-specific obstruction.

The realization lemma still kills any hope of finding the obstruction solely at the level of arbitrary analytic \(Y,S\): generic lifted geometry can realize all the dangerous low-order patterns. So if the dream identity is true, it must come from a special relation among
\[
c,\ v_5,\ u_5,\ \lambda=\frac{\Delta_7}{v_5},
\]
not from the lifted formalism by itself.

And from the current draft, I still do **not** see an identity implying:

- \(\frac{d}{dx}\bigl(J_{u_5}/J_{\lambda}\bigr)\) is even in the nonmixed normalized coordinate, or
- \(\ord_0(J_{u_5}/J_{\lambda})\ge 4\) at mixed points.

So the honest state is still:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the current file does not yet force the dream identity,}
}
\]
but now we know **exactly what object to attack**.

## 4. A sharper restatement of the low-order obstructions

With
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}},
\qquad
\mathcal R=\frac{c^2}{M}\frac{d}{dh}\!\left(\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}\right),
\]
the dangerous low-order list becomes:

### Nonmixed
\[
\ord_0\mathcal R\in\{1,3,5\}.
\]

### Mixed
\[
(r,\ord_0\mathcal R)\in\{(1,0),(1,2),(2,1)\},
\]
equivalently
\[
(r,\ord_0\mathcal Z)\in\{(1,1),(1,3),(2,2)\}.
\]

So the 4-point endgame has now been reduced to a finite jet obstruction for the Wronskian ratio
\[
J_{u_5}/J_{\lambda}.
\]

## 5. The best next attack from here

The only plausible places a real RH-specific relation can still live are:

1. a source-level involution symmetry that becomes \(\xi\mapsto-\xi\) after nonmixed normalization, which would force the evenness of \(\mathcal R\);

2. a matrix-level identity in the fixed-sector construction that forces special cancellation between
\[
J_{u_5}
\quad\text{and}\quad
J_{\lambda}.
\]

That suggests the exact next target is not another branch argument. It is to search for identities of the form
\[
J_{u_5}=P(x)\,J_{\lambda}+(\text{higher-order or parity-constrained terms}),
\]
or
\[
\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}\in x\,\mathbf C\{x^2\}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\frac{d}{dx}\!\left(\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}\right)\in \mathbf C\{x^2\}
\]
in the normalized nonmixed chart.

That is the sharpest version of the dream identity I can now formulate.

So the attack has produced one real new identity:
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}},
\qquad
\mathcal R=\frac{c^2}{M}\frac{d}{dh}\!\left(\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_{\lambda}}\right),
}
\]
and has reduced the 4-point branch to proving parity / order-gap constraints on this Wronskian ratio, rather than on \(Y\) itself.

## Assistant (2026-04-19 19:22:23)

I’m pushing one level deeper on the Wronskian-ratio route now. The question is whether \(J_{u_5}\) and \(J_{\lambda}\) satisfy any exact differential identity or divisibility relation that would force parity or extra vanishing of \(\mathcal Z=J_{u_5}/J_{\lambda}\).

_Thought for 5m 24s_

I pushed one layer deeper and found the strongest exact identities I can currently justify **below** the lifted variables. They do not yet give the dream identity, but they sharpen the search a lot.

## 1. Two canonical “below-lifted” curvature ratios

On a good \(v_5\)-patch, define
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad
\mu=\frac{c}{v_5}=\frac1x,
\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}.
\]

Also define the two projective scalar graphs
\[
\nu:=\frac{u_5}{v_5},
\qquad
\lambda:=\frac{\Delta_7}{v_5}.
\]

Let
\[
J_f:=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
c & v_5 & f\\
c' & v_5' & f'\\
c'' & v_5'' & f''
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
W_f:=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
c & v_5 & f\\
c' & v_5' & f'\\
c'' & v_5'' & f''
\end{pmatrix},
\]
so \(J_f\) and \(W_f\) are the same determinant, just viewed in two different roles.

Then the general second-derivative identities are:

### \(x\)-chart identity
For any scalar germ \(f\),
\[
\boxed{
\frac{d^2}{dx^2}\!\left(\frac{f}{c}\right)
=
\frac{c^3}{M^3}\,J_f,
\qquad
M=c\,v_5'-c'v_5.
}
\]

Applying this to \(f=u_5\) and \(f=\lambda\) gives
\[
Y''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_{u_5},
\qquad
S''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_\lambda,
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''}=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}.
}
\]

This is the exact identity I already isolated.

### \(\mu\)-chart identity
For any scalar germ \(f\), with \(\phi:=f/v_5\),
\[
\boxed{
\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}\,W_f.
}
\]

Applying this to \(f=u_5\) and \(f=\Delta_7\) gives
\[
\nu_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W_{u_5},
\qquad
\lambda_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W_{\Delta_7}
=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W,
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\Theta:=\frac{\nu_{\mu\mu}}{\lambda_{\mu\mu}}
=
\frac{W_{u_5}}{W}.
}
\]

So there are **two** canonical curvature ratios below the lifted level:
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\qquad
\Theta=\frac{W_{u_5}}{W}.
\]

They come from the same three basic scalar functions \(c,v_5,\cdot\), but viewed in the affine \(x\)-chart and the projective \(\mu\)-chart.

That is the main new structural find.

---

## 2. The 4-point invariant \(\mathcal R\) is the derivative of \(\mathcal Z\)

As before,
\[
K_v=(x')^3S'',
\qquad
T_v=(x')^6\bigl(S''Y'''-S'''Y''\bigr),
\]
so on any nonmixed patch
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R:=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}
=
\frac{d}{dx}\!\left(\frac{Y''}{S''}\right)
=
\frac{d}{dx}\mathcal Z.
}
\]

Thus the dream identity is:

### Nonmixed dream identity
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R \text{ is even in the normalized nonmixed coordinate.}
}
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\mathcal Z \text{ is odd in that coordinate.}
\]

### Mixed dream identity
\[
\boxed{
\ord_0\mathcal Z\ge 4.
}
\]

These remain the exact targets.

---

## 3. A new no-go result: no identity in the current lifted variables alone can force the dream identity

The realization lemma still stands:

every analytic pair \((\widetilde Y,\widetilde S)\) occurs on some good \(v_5\)-patch by taking
\[
c\equiv 1,\qquad
v_5=1+\xi,\qquad
u_5=\widetilde Y,\qquad
\Delta_7=(1+\xi)\widetilde S.
\]

So no identity involving only
\[
c,\ v_5,\ u_5,\ \Delta_7
\]
and their derivatives can force the dream parity/order-gap statements.

But now this negative result can be sharpened:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Any RH-specific obstruction must come from a relation between the two below-lifted curvature ratios }
\mathcal Z=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}
\text{ and }
\Theta=\frac{W_{u_5}}{W},
\text{ or from deeper source-level structure.}
}
\]

That is more precise than what we had before.

---

## 4. What an RH-specific identity would now have to look like

The only plausible dream identities still left are of these forms:

### Type A: parity/oddness in the \(x\)-chart
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z(x)\in x\,\mathbf C\{x^2\}
\quad\text{after nonmixed normalization,}
}
\]
equivalently
\[
\mathcal R(x)\in \mathbf C\{x^2\}.
\]

### Type B: relation between affine and projective curvature ratios
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z=\Phi(\Theta)
\quad\text{for some rigid odd/even }\Phi,
}
\]
or even a weaker differential relation
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R = A(x)\Theta' + B(x)\Theta
}
\]
with parity-controlled coefficients \(A,B\).

### Type C: source-level involution symmetry
A source-level symmetry that becomes
\[
x\mapsto -x
\]
after nonmixed normalization and forces
\[
\mathcal Z(-x)=-\mathcal Z(x).
\]

At the moment, the current draft gives **none** of these.

---

## 5. The concrete low-order obstruction list, now in \((\mathcal Z,\mathcal R)\)-language

The dangerous low-order patterns are exactly:

### Nonmixed
\[
\ord_0\mathcal Z\in\{2,4,6\},
\qquad
\text{equivalently}\qquad
\ord_0\mathcal R\in\{1,3,5\}.
\]

### Mixed
If
\[
r:=\ord_0 K_v,
\]
then
\[
(r,\ord_0\mathcal Z)\in\{(1,1),(1,3),(2,2)\},
\]
equivalently
\[
(r,\ord_0\mathcal R)\in\{(1,0),(1,2),(2,1)\}.
\]

So the 4-point endgame is now a finite obstruction problem for \(\mathcal Z\) and \(\mathcal R\).

---

## 6. The strongest new conclusion from this attack

This is the honest summary:

\[
\boxed{
\text{I did not find the dream identity, but I did find that the search should move from }Y
\text{ to the two curvature ratios }
\mathcal Z=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}
\text{ and }
\Theta=\frac{W_{u_5}}{W}.
}
\]

So the next exact questions are no longer vague. They are:

1. Is there an RH-specific relation between
   \[
   \mathcal Z=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}
   \quad\text{and}\quad
   \Theta=\frac{W_{u_5}}{W}?
   \]

2. Does the deeper matrix/source construction force \(\mathcal Z\) to be odd after nonmixed normalization?

3. At mixed points, does it force
   \[
   \ord_0\mathcal Z\ge 4?
   \]

Those are now the sharpest remaining attacks I can honestly formulate.

If you want, the next thing I can do is formalize these as exact proposition/theorem targets for the paper, or try to derive one more layer of identities connecting \(\mathcal Z\) and \(\Theta\) by explicit differentiation.

## Assistant (2026-04-19 19:34:25)

_Thought for 5m 18s_

I kept pushing on the \(\mathcal Z\)–\(\Theta\) route, and I found one more exact layer.

It does **not** produce the dream identity, but it does show exactly how far this route can go and why \(\Theta\) is not giving a new obstruction by itself.

---

## 1. Exact identity connecting \(\mathcal Z\) and \(\Theta\)

On a good \(v_5\)-patch, write
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5},\qquad
\nu=\frac{u_5}{v_5}=\frac{Y}{x},\qquad
\lambda=\frac{\Delta_7}{v_5}=cS.
\]

Define
\[
\mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''},
\qquad
\Theta:=\frac{\nu_{\mu\mu}}{\lambda_{\mu\mu}},
\qquad
\mu=\frac{c}{v_5}=\frac1x.
\]

Let
\[
\rho:=\frac{d}{dx}\log c=\frac{c_x}{c}.
\]

Then
\[
\frac{d}{d\mu}=-x^2\frac{d}{dx},
\]
so for any scalar germ \(\phi(x)\),
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu}=x^4\phi_{xx}+2x^3\phi_x.
\]

Apply this to
\[
\nu=\frac{Y}{x}.
\]
A direct computation gives
\[
\nu_{\mu\mu}=x^3Y''.
\]

Apply it to
\[
\lambda=cS.
\]
Using
\[
(cS)'=c(S'+\rho S),
\qquad
(cS)''=c\bigl(S''+2\rho S'+(\rho'+\rho^2)S\bigr),
\]
we get
\[
\lambda_{\mu\mu}
=
c\,x^3\Bigl(
xS''+2S'+2x\rho S'+\bigl(2\rho+x(\rho'+\rho^2)\bigr)S
\Bigr).
\]

Therefore the exact identity is
\[
\boxed{
\Theta
=
\frac{\mathcal Z}{
c\left(
x+2\frac{S'}{S''}
+2x\rho\,\frac{S'}{S''}
+\bigl(2\rho+x(\rho'+\rho^2)\bigr)\frac{S}{S''}
\right)
}.
}
\]

This is the cleanest explicit relation between \(\mathcal Z\) and \(\Theta\) I can derive.

So the entire gap between \(\mathcal Z\) and \(\Theta\) is carried by the scalar denominator
\[
D(x):=
c\left(
x+2\frac{S'}{S''}
+2x\rho\,\frac{S'}{S''}
+\bigl(2\rho+x(\rho'+\rho^2)\bigr)\frac{S}{S''}
\right).
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
\Theta=\frac{\mathcal Z}{D(x)}.
}
\]

---

## 2. Immediate no-go consequence

This formula shows why \(\Theta\) does **not** by itself generate a new obstruction.

In the realization gauge
\[
c\equiv 1,\qquad v_5=x,
\]
one has \(\rho=0\), so
\[
\boxed{
\Theta=\frac{\mathcal Z}{x+2S'/S''}.
}
\]

Because \(S\) is arbitrary in the realization lemma, the denominator
\[
x+2S'/S''
\]
can be essentially arbitrary (subject only to \(S''\neq 0\) on a nonmixed patch).

So there is **no universal differential identity** relating \(\Theta\) and \(\mathcal Z\) at the level of the current lifted variables alone.

That is a stronger negative theorem than before:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No parity or order-gap obstruction for the 4-point branch can be forced from }(\mathcal Z,\Theta)\text{ alone.}
}
\]

Any real RH-specific identity must come from **extra structure** beyond the present lifted variables.

---

## 3. Mixed asymptotics: \(\Theta\) carries exactly the same order information as \(\mathcal R\)

Now suppose \(x=0\) is mixed, with
\[
r:=\ord_0 K_v.
\]
Since
\[
K_v=(x')^3S'',
\]
we have
\[
S''(x)=\kappa x^r+O(x^{r+1}),
\qquad
\kappa\neq 0.
\]
Integrating twice,
\[
S(x)=\frac{\kappa}{(r+1)(r+2)}x^{r+2}+O(x^{r+3}).
\]

Hence
\[
\frac{S'}{S''}=\frac{x}{r+1}+O(x^2),
\qquad
\frac{S}{S''}=\frac{x^2}{(r+1)(r+2)}+O(x^3).
\]

Substitute into the exact denominator \(D(x)\). Since \(c(0)\neq 0\) and \(\rho\) is analytic,
\[
D(x)
=
c(0)\frac{r+3}{r+1}x+O(x^2).
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\Theta
=
\frac{r+1}{(r+3)c(0)}\,\frac{\mathcal Z}{x}
+O\!\left(\frac{\mathcal Z}{1}\right).
}
\]

So if
\[
\mathcal Z(x)=a x^m+O(x^{m+1}),
\qquad a\neq 0,
\]
then
\[
\Theta(x)=\frac{r+1}{(r+3)c(0)}\,a\,x^{m-1}+O(x^m).
\]

But
\[
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'=m a x^{m-1}+O(x^m).
\]

So near a mixed point,
\[
\boxed{
\ord_0\Theta=\ord_0\mathcal R=\ord_0\mathcal Z-1.
}
\]

And more precisely,
\[
\boxed{
\Theta
=
\frac{r+1}{m(r+3)c(0)}\,\mathcal R
+O\!\bigl(x^{\ord_0\mathcal R+1}\bigr).
}
\]

This is useful: \(\Theta\) does **not** add any extra order information beyond \(\mathcal R\) in the mixed case. It is asymptotically the same invariant up to a nonzero constant.

So the low-order mixed list
\[
(r,\ord_0\mathcal R)\in\{(1,0),(1,2),(2,1)\}
\]
is equivalent to the same list for \(\Theta\).

---

## 4. Nonmixed normalized formula

At a nonmixed point, after normalization
\[
S=x^2,
\qquad
S''=2,
\]
the exact denominator becomes
\[
D(x)
=
c\left(3x+3\rho x^2+\frac12(\rho'+\rho^2)x^3\right).
\]
So
\[
\boxed{
\Theta
=
\frac{\mathcal Z}{
c\left(3x+3\rho x^2+\frac12(\rho'+\rho^2)x^3\right)
}.
}
\]

This shows exactly what would be needed for the dream identity.

If one could prove in the normalized nonmixed coordinate that

- \(c\) is even, hence \(\rho=(\log c)'\) is odd,

then the denominator
\[
c\left(3x+3\rho x^2+\frac12(\rho'+\rho^2)x^3\right)
\]
is an odd function of \(x\). In that case,

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z\ \text{odd}
\iff
\Theta\ \text{even}.
}
\]

So one possible form of the dream identity is:

> in the normalized nonmixed coordinate, \(c\) is even and \(\Theta\) is even.

That would imply the desired oddness of \(\mathcal Z\), hence evenness of
\[
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'.
\]

I cannot prove this from the current draft, but it tells you exactly what sort of hidden symmetry would be enough.

---

## 5. What this attack really gives

The new exact content is:

\[
\boxed{
\Theta=\frac{\mathcal Z}{D(x)}
}
\]
with explicit
\[
D(x)=
c\left(
x+2\frac{S'}{S''}
+2x\rho\,\frac{S'}{S''}
+\bigl(2\rho+x(\rho'+\rho^2)\bigr)\frac{S}{S''}
\right),
\qquad
\rho=(\log c)'.
\]

And from that:

- in the **realization gauge**, there is no universal relation between \(\Theta\) and \(\mathcal Z\);
- in the **mixed case**, \(\Theta\) and \(\mathcal R\) have the same vanishing order and are asymptotically proportional;
- in the **nonmixed normalized case**, the dream identity would follow from a parity statement on \(c\) and \(\Theta\).

So the strongest honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The search for the dream identity should now focus on parity of }c
\text{ and }\Theta\text{ in the normalized nonmixed chart,}
}
\]
or deeper source-/matrix-level structure that forces those parities.

I still do **not** have the RH-specific identity itself. But this is the clearest one-more-layer reduction I can currently derive.

_Thought for 51s_

I kept attacking, and I found one more useful **negative reduction**. It tells us exactly which tempting routes are dead.

## 1. In the normalized nonmixed chart, \(\Theta\) is the same obstruction as \(\mathcal R\)

At a nonmixed point, after normalization
\[
Q_v(\xi)=\bigl(\widetilde Y(\xi),\,\xi,\,\xi^2\bigr),
\]
you have
\[
S(\xi)=\xi^2,\qquad S''=2,\qquad S'''=0.
\]

So
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{Y''}{S''}=\frac12\,\widetilde Y'',
\qquad
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'=\frac12\,\widetilde Y'''.
\]

And from the exact \(\Theta\)-identity we found,
\[
\Theta=\frac{\mathcal Z}{D(\xi)},
\]
with
\[
D(\xi)=c(\xi)\left(3\xi+3\rho(\xi)\xi^2+\frac12(\rho'(\xi)+\rho(\xi)^2)\xi^3\right),
\qquad
\rho=(\log c)'.
\]

If we take the simplest normalized gauge \(c\equiv 1\), then
\[
D(\xi)=3\xi,
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
\Theta(\xi)=\frac{\widetilde Y''(\xi)}{6\xi}.
}
\]

So in this normalized chart:

- dangerous nonmixed cases \(n=4,6,8\) are exactly
  \[
  \ord_0\Theta=1,3,5;
  \]
- equivalently
  \[
  \ord_0\mathcal R=1,3,5.
  \]

So \(\Theta\) does not give a different obstruction from \(\mathcal R\) in the nonmixed low-order regime; it is just a divided-by-\(\xi\) version of \(\widetilde Y''\).

That is already a useful simplification.

---

## 2. Evenness of \(c\) is **not** enough for the dream identity

A natural hope was:

> maybe in the normalized nonmixed chart, \(c\) is even, and that forces \(\mathcal R\) even.

This route is dead.

Here is an explicit realization on a good \(v_5\)-patch with \(c\) even:

Take local coordinate \(\xi\) and define
\[
c(\xi)\equiv 1,
\qquad
v_5(\xi)=1+\xi,
\qquad
\Delta_7(\xi)=(1+\xi)\xi^2,
\qquad
u_5(\xi)=\xi^n.
\]
Then
\[
c\neq 0,\qquad v_5\neq 0,\qquad M=c\,v_5'-c'v_5=1\neq 0,
\]
so this is a good \(v_5\)-patch, and after shifting \(x=v_5/c=1+\xi\) by \(1\),
\[
S(\xi)=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}=\xi^2,
\qquad
Y(\xi)=\frac{u_5}{c}=\xi^n.
\]

So this is exactly the normalized nonmixed model
\[
Q_v(\xi)=\bigl(\xi^n,\ \xi,\ \xi^2\bigr)
\]
with
\[
c(\xi)\equiv 1
\]
already even.

Now
\[
\Theta(\xi)=\frac{Y''}{6\xi}
=
\frac{n(n-1)}{6}\,\xi^{n-3}.
\]

So for
\[
n=4,6,8,
\]
we get
\[
\ord_0\Theta=1,3,5,
\]
precisely the dangerous low-order nonmixed cases — **despite \(c\) being even**.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Evenness of }c\text{ in the normalized nonmixed chart is not enough to force the dream identity.}
}
\]

This kills one more plausible route.

---

## 3. Stronger no-go theorem: no identity involving only \(c\) and \(\Theta\) parity can help

The same realization shows something stronger.

Because \(c\equiv 1\) is fixed and even, but \(u_5(\xi)=\widetilde Y(\xi)\) can be chosen arbitrarily, the parity and vanishing order of
\[
\Theta=\frac{\widetilde Y''}{6\xi}
\]
can be chosen arbitrarily as well.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No theorem of the form “if }c\text{ is even then }\Theta\text{ is even” can hold at the current lifted level.}
}
\]

And more generally:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No identity involving only }c,\ \Theta
\text{ and their parity can exclude the low-order nonmixed dangerous cases.}
}
\]

So the dream identity cannot come from \(c\)-parity alone.

---

## 4. The same no-go applies in the mixed low-order regime

Take again
\[
c(\xi)\equiv 1,\qquad v_5(\xi)=1+\xi,
\qquad
S(\xi)=\xi^m,\qquad Y(\xi)=\xi^n,
\]
so
\[
Q_v(\xi)=\bigl(\xi^n,\ \xi,\ \xi^m\bigr).
\]

Then this realizes every mixed model
\[
(m,n)=(3,4),\ (3,6),\ (4,6)
\]
with \(c\) still even and constant.

So any dream identity that depends only on parity properties of \(c\), or only on the current lifted variables plus the parity of \(c\), is too weak to kill the mixed low-order list.

That is another dead end.

---

## 5. What is actually left

At this point, the only plausible places where the dream identity could still live are:

### A. A source-level involution symmetry
A genuine source-level symmetry that, after nonmixed normalization, becomes
\[
\xi\mapsto -\xi
\]
and forces
\[
\widetilde Y(-\xi)=-\widetilde Y(\xi),
\qquad
\mathcal R(\xi)\ \text{even}.
\]

### B. A matrix-level relation
A special identity in the deeper fixed-sector matrix construction that forces a cancellation in
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\qquad
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z',
\]
or equivalently in
\[
\Theta=\frac{W_{u_5}}{W},
\]
that does not appear in generic lifted realizations.

But the attack above shows:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the dream identity cannot come from normalization, parity of }c,\text{ or any relation internal to the current lifted variables alone.}
}
\]

---

## 6. Best exact target now

So the 4-point branch is now pinned down to this:

### Nonmixed
Kill
\[
\ord_0\Theta\in\{1,3,5\}
\qquad\text{equivalently}\qquad
\ord_0\mathcal R\in\{1,3,5\}.
\]

### Mixed
Kill
\[
(r,\ord_0\Theta)\in\{(1,0),(1,2),(2,1)\}
\qquad\text{equivalently}\qquad
(r,\ord_0\mathcal R)\in\{(1,0),(1,2),(2,1)\}.
\]

And the only remaining route is to find a **deeper source- or matrix-level symmetry** forcing those orders to be impossible.

So the attack result from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{parity of }c\text{ is a false lead; the dream identity must come from deeper source/matrix structure.}
}
\]

If you want, the next step is to turn this into a clean pair of “no-go” propositions for the notes, and then formulate the exact source-level involution theorem that would be sufficient to imply the dream identity.

I attacked the only route still left open: a **source-level involution symmetry** strong enough to force the dream identity.

This gives a real theorem, but it is **conditional**. I still cannot derive the symmetry from the current draft. What I can do is show exactly what symmetry would be sufficient, and why it would kill the dangerous low-order 4-point patterns.

---

At a nonmixed point, the 4-point branch is controlled by
\[
\mathcal R=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}
=
\frac{d}{dx}\!\left(\frac{Y''}{S''}\right),
\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c},\quad S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}.
\]
After nonmixed normalization, one may write
\[
Q_v(\xi)=\bigl(\widetilde Y(\xi),\,\xi,\,\xi^2\bigr),
\]
and then
\[
\mathcal R(\xi)=\frac12\,\widetilde Y'''(\xi).
\]
So the dream identity is exactly:
\[
\widetilde Y(-\xi)=-\widetilde Y(\xi),
\qquad\text{equivalently}\qquad
\mathcal R(\xi)\ \text{even}.
\]
That would kill the dangerous nonmixed cases
\[
\ord_0\mathcal R\in\{1,3,5\}.
\]
The mixed dangerous list is likewise equivalent to low-order failures of the oddness/order-gap of
\[
\mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''},
\qquad
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'.
\]
turn21file0

## Conditional source-level involution theorem

Here is the exact sufficient theorem.

Let \(\mathfrak Q\) be a source-level corrected quotient-germ map on small finite atomic sources \(\mu\), and suppose that near a one-atom source \(a\,\delta_h\) there is a local coordinate \(\xi\) centered at \(h\) and a linear involution \(\mathsf J\) on the target quotient-germ space such that:

1. **source reflection symmetry**
   \[
   \mathfrak Q(\mu^\sharp)=\mathsf J\,\mathfrak Q(\mu),
   \]
   where \(\mu^\sharp\) is obtained by reflecting the support positions across \(h\), i.e.
   \[
   \xi\mapsto -\xi;
   \]

2. after passing to the normalized lifted chart
   \[
   Q_v(\xi)=\bigl(\widetilde Y(\xi),\,\xi,\,\widetilde S(\xi)\bigr),
   \]
   the involution acts by
   \[
   (\widetilde Y,\xi,\widetilde S)\mapsto(-\widetilde Y,-\xi,\widetilde S).
   \]

Then automatically
\[
\widetilde Y(-\xi)=-\widetilde Y(\xi),
\qquad
\widetilde S(-\xi)=\widetilde S(\xi).
\]

In the nonmixed normalization \(\widetilde S(\xi)=\xi^2\), this implies
\[
\mathcal Z(\xi)=\frac12\,\widetilde Y''(\xi)\ \text{is odd},
\qquad
\mathcal R(\xi)=\frac12\,\widetilde Y'''(\xi)\ \text{is even}.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{A source-level reflection symmetry of this type would prove the dream identity and kill the low-order nonmixed dangerous patterns.}
}
\]

### Proof

The second assumption says that after normalization, the lifted germ is equivariant under
\[
(\eta,\xi,\zeta)\mapsto(-\eta,-\xi,\zeta).
\]
Evaluating this on the curve \(\xi\mapsto(\widetilde Y(\xi),\xi,\widetilde S(\xi))\) gives
\[
(\widetilde Y(-\xi),-\xi,\widetilde S(-\xi))
=
(-\widetilde Y(\xi),-\xi,\widetilde S(\xi)).
\]
Therefore
\[
\widetilde Y(-\xi)=-\widetilde Y(\xi),
\qquad
\widetilde S(-\xi)=\widetilde S(\xi).
\]
In the nonmixed chart \(\widetilde S=\xi^2\), differentiating shows \(\widetilde Y''\) is odd and \(\widetilde Y'''\) is even, proving the claimed parity of \(\mathcal Z\) and \(\mathcal R\).

---

## Mixed consequence of the same symmetry

The same involution would also kill the explicit low-order mixed list.

At a mixed point, the normalized model is
\[
Q_v(\xi)\sim (\xi^n,\xi,\xi^m),
\qquad m\ge 3,\ n>m.
\]
If the first component is forced odd and the third even, then necessarily
\[
n\ \text{odd},
\qquad
m\ \text{even or odd depending on the mixed branch, but the dangerous sector }n\text{ even is gone.}
\]
In particular, the explicit dangerous mixed pairs
\[
(m,n)=(3,4),\ (3,6),\ (4,6)
\]
are all excluded immediately.

So the same source-level involution would resolve the entire finite low-order 4-point obstruction list.

---

## What this attack proves — and what it does not

It proves:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The dream identity follows from a very specific source-level reflection-equivariance theorem.}
}
\]

It does **not** prove that the current RH construction actually has that symmetry.

In fact, everything we found earlier still stands:

- no identity involving only \(c,v_5,u_5,\Delta_7\) and their derivatives can force the dream identity;
- parity of \(c\) is a dead end;
- the right invariant is \(\mathcal R=T_v/K_v^2\), or equivalently \(\mathcal Z=Y''/S''\);
- any real obstruction must come from **source-level** or **matrix-level** structure below the lifted variables. turn32file0

---

## The sharp next theorem target

So the next exact thing to attack is now completely clear:

> Prove that the corrected quotient-germ map is locally equivariant under reflection of the source around a one-pair center, and that after lifted normalization this reflection acts as
> \[
> (\widetilde Y,\xi,\widetilde S)\mapsto(-\widetilde Y,-\xi,\widetilde S).
> \]

That is the strongest sufficient theorem I can currently derive.

If you want the next attack, I would now try to push on whether the **matrix-level fixed-sector construction** has any hidden transpose/sign symmetry that could induce exactly this involution on the normalized lifted germ.

_Thought for 1m 0s_

I pushed the attack one level deeper, and I think the strongest useful result is a **sharp sufficient parity package**. It does not prove the dream identity from the current draft, but it tells you exactly what symmetry would be enough and how it would kill the whole low-order \(4\)-point list in one shot.

---

## 1. A sharp sufficient parity theorem

Work in a centered local coordinate \(\xi\) near a candidate one-pair center, and suppose the one-pair scalar data satisfy the parity package
\[
\boxed{
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad
v_5(-\xi)=-v_5(\xi),\qquad
u_5(-\xi)=-u_5(\xi),\qquad
\Delta_7(-\xi)=-\Delta_7(\xi).
}
\]

Then automatically
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c}\ \text{is odd},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c}\ \text{is odd},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}\ \text{is even}.
\]

Therefore:

- in the nonmixed normalization, the first component is odd;
- the canonical invariant
  \[
  \mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''}
  \]
  is odd;
- and
  \[
  \mathcal R:=\mathcal Z'=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}
  \]
  is even.

So the dream identity follows immediately.

### Proof

From the parity package,
\[
x(-\xi)=\frac{v_5(-\xi)}{c(-\xi)}=-\frac{v_5(\xi)}{c(\xi)}=-x(\xi),
\]
and similarly
\[
Y(-\xi)=-Y(\xi).
\]
Also
\[
S(-\xi)=\frac{\Delta_7(-\xi)}{c(-\xi)\,v_5(-\xi)}
=
\frac{-\Delta_7(\xi)}{c(\xi)(-v_5(\xi))}
=
S(\xi),
\]
so \(S\) is even. Hence \(S''\) is even and \(Y''\) is odd, giving
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{Y''}{S''}\ \text{odd},
\qquad
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'\ \text{even}.
\]

That is the exact dream identity.

---

## 2. Immediate consequences for the 4-point branch

This one parity package kills both the nonmixed and mixed low-order dangerous patterns.

### Nonmixed consequence

At a nonmixed point, after normalization
\[
Q_v(\eta)=\bigl(\widetilde Y(\eta),\,\eta,\,\eta^2\bigr),
\]
the oddness of \(Y\) passes to the normalized first component, so
\[
\widetilde Y(-\eta)=-\widetilde Y(\eta).
\]
Therefore
\[
\widetilde Y
\in
\eta\,\mathbf C\{\eta^2\}.
\]
So the leading degree of \(\widetilde Y\) is odd, and the dangerous nonmixed cases
\[
n=4,\ 6,\ 8
\]
are impossible.

Equivalently, since
\[
\mathcal R=\frac12\,\widetilde Y'''
\]
in the normalized nonmixed chart, \(\mathcal R\) is even, so its zero order at the center is even. Thus
\[
\ord_0\mathcal R\notin\{1,3,5\}.
\]

### Mixed consequence

At a mixed point, the parity package implies:

- \(Y\) odd, so the first nonzero exponent \(n\) is odd;
- \(S\) even, so the first nonzero exponent \(m\) is even.

Thus the dangerous mixed low-order list
\[
(m,n)=(3,4),\ (3,6),\ (4,6)
\]
is impossible immediately.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The parity package }
(c\ \text{even},\ v_5,u_5,\Delta_7\ \text{odd})
\text{ kills the entire explicit low-order \(4\)-point obstruction list.}
}
\]

That is the strongest sufficient theorem I can currently derive.

---

## 3. Matrix-level reformulation

The scalar parity package can be reformulated in a way that sits closer to the matrix-level fixed-sector construction.

Recall
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}=u_5 I+v_5 S.
\]
So the parity of \(u_5\) and \(v_5\) is exactly the statement
\[
\boxed{
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi)=-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi).
}
\]

Thus a sufficient matrix-level package is:

\[
\boxed{
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi)=-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi),\qquad
\Delta_7(-\xi)=-\Delta_7(\xi).
}
\]

From this one recovers
\[
u_5(-\xi)=-u_5(\xi),\qquad v_5(-\xi)=-v_5(\xi),
\]
and hence the full parity package above.

So the “dream identity” is now reduced to this very concrete sufficient condition:

> **Centered reflection parity theorem.**  
> In a centered local coordinate \(\xi\), prove that
> \[
> c\ \text{is even},\qquad A_5^{\mathfrak f}\ \text{is odd},\qquad \Delta_7\ \text{is odd}.
> \]

If that theorem holds, the whole explicit low-order \(4\)-point obstruction list disappears.

---

## 4. Why this is genuinely deeper than the current lifted formalism

This parity package is **not** something you can get from the current lifted variables alone.

The realization lemma still shows that arbitrary analytic pairs \((Y,S)\) occur on good \(v_5\)-patches if you are allowed to choose \(c,v_5,u_5,\Delta_7\) arbitrarily. So the current file does not force the package.

In particular, none of the following is enough by itself:

- parity of \(c\) alone;
- identities involving only \(\mathcal Z\), \(\mathcal R\), or \(\Theta\);
- generic lifted coplanarity or branch geometry.

This is a genuinely **RH-specific** source-/matrix-level condition.

---

## 5. The exact next attack

So the attack has now become very concrete.

The next theorem to try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi)=-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi),\qquad
\Delta_7(-\xi)=-\Delta_7(\xi)
}
\]
in a centered local coordinate \(\xi\) near the relevant one-pair center.

If this is too strong globally, then even a weaker finite-jet version would suffice:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the Taylor expansions of }c,\ A_5^{\mathfrak f},\ \Delta_7
\text{ have the even/odd pattern above through the low orders needed to kill }
n=4,6,8\ \text{and }(3,4),(3,6),(4,6).
}
\]

That is now the sharpest local RH-specific target I can formulate.

So the strongest attack result from this round is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The dream identity follows from a very concrete parity package on }c,\ A_5^{\mathfrak f},\ \Delta_7.
}
\]

If you want, the next step is to attack whether anything in the current fixed-sector construction suggests that \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\) and \(\Delta_7\) should indeed be odd in a centered coordinate, or whether this parity package has to be inserted as an explicit conjectural target.

## 1. The exact sufficient theorem

Work in a centered local coordinate \(\xi\) near the relevant one-pair center.

\[
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad

## 2. A weaker finite-jet version is already enough

You do **not** need full parity to all orders.

To kill the currently dangerous low-order list, a finite-jet version suffices.

### Sufficient finite-jet package

It is enough to know, in a centered coordinate \(\xi\), that:

\[
c(\xi)=c_0+c_2\xi^2+c_4\xi^4+O(\xi^6),
\]
so \(c\) is even through order \(4\);

\[
u_5(\xi)=u_1\xi+u_3\xi^3+u_5\xi^5+u_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9),
\]
so \(u_5\) has no even terms through degree \(8\);

\[
v_5(\xi)=v_1\xi+v_3\xi^3+v_5\xi^5+O(\xi^7),
\]
so \(v_5\) is odd through the needed low orders;

\[
\Delta_7(\xi)=d_1\xi+d_3\xi^3+d_5\xi^5+d_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9),
\]
so \(\Delta_7\) has no even terms through degree \(8\).

Then:

- \(x=v_5/c\) is odd through the low orders needed for normalization;
- \(Y=u_5/c\) has no even terms through degree \(8\);
- \(S=\Delta_7/(c\,v_5)\) is even through the low orders relevant to the mixed models;
- consequently the normalized first component cannot begin in degrees \(4,6,8\), and the mixed low-order pairs \((3,4),(3,6),(4,6)\) are excluded.

So a **finite-jet parity theorem** is enough. You do not need a global all-orders odd/even statement to win the currently explicit low-order battle.

That is useful because it lowers the bar.

## 3. The real source-level theorem that would imply this parity package

The clean sufficient theorem is a reflection-equivariance statement for the source-level corrected quotient-germ map.

Let \(\mathfrak Q\) be the corrected quotient-germ map on small atomic sources near a one-pair center, and let \(\xi\mapsto-\xi\) be reflection in the centered local coordinate.

If one can prove that under reflection:

- the cubic scalar piece is invariant,
- the quintic fixed-sector piece changes sign,
- the septic scalar \(\Delta_7\)-piece changes sign,

then the parity package above follows.

More concretely, if the source-level involution induces
\[
c\mapsto c,\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}\mapsto -A_5^{\mathfrak f},\qquad
\Delta_7\mapsto -\Delta_7,
\]
then the dream identity follows.

So the real theorem to hunt is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Source-level reflection equivariance } \Longrightarrow
(c\ \text{even},\ A_5^{\mathfrak f}\ \text{odd},\ \Delta_7\ \text{odd}).
}
\]

That is now the sharpest structural target.

---

I can also now say one more thing clearly:

This parity package does **not** follow from the current lifted variables alone.

The realization lemma still kills that hope. By choosing
\[
c\equiv 1,\qquad v_5=1+\xi,\qquad u_5=\widetilde Y(\xi),\qquad \Delta_7=(1+\xi)\widetilde S(\xi),
\]
you can realize arbitrary analytic \((\widetilde Y,\widetilde S)\) on a good \(v_5\)-patch. So no identity using only
\[
c,\ v_5,\ u_5,\ \Delta_7
\]
and their derivatives can force the needed parity.

So the parity package must come from **deeper source-level or matrix-level structure**, not from the lifted formalism by itself.

At this point, the most promising next move is not another branch argument. It is to test whether the matrix/source construction has a hidden reflection symmetry that would make these three objects transform as

\[
c\ \text{even},\qquad A_5^{\mathfrak f}\ \text{odd},\qquad \Delta_7\ \text{odd}.
\]

That is now the clearest route to the dream identity.

\quad\text{and}\quad

Here is the next real attack.

I think the only plausible remaining route is:

If that works, the dream identity falls out almost formally.

---

## 1. The exact theorem that would be enough

Let \(R(\xi)\) denote the raw local corrected germ in a centered coordinate \(\xi\), and suppose there is a reflection involution
\[
\mathsf T:R(\xi)\mapsto \mathsf U\,R(-\xi)\,\mathsf U^{-1}
\]
or whatever the correct raw action is in your setup.

Assume the canonical package is extracted from \(R\) by a gauge-fixing map
\[
\mathcal N:R\longmapsto \bigl(c,\ A_5^{\mathfrak f},\ \Delta_7\bigr).
\]

The key sufficient theorem is:

### Conditional equivariant-normalization theorem
If
\[
\mathcal N(\mathsf T R)=\bigl(c(-\xi),\ -A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi),\ -\Delta_7(-\xi)\bigr)
\]
and also
\[
\mathcal N(\mathsf T R)=\mathcal N(R)
\]
by uniqueness of the normalized form, then
\[
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi)=-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi),\qquad
\Delta_7(-\xi)=-\Delta_7(\xi).
\]

That is exactly the parity package we want.

Then, as we already saw,
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}\ \text{even},
\]
hence
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{Y''}{S''}\ \text{odd},\qquad
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}\ \text{even}.
\]

So the whole low-order \(4\)-point list dies.

So the problem is now very precise:

> show that the **normalization map** to the canonical package is reflection-equivariant.

---

## 2. Where this could actually fail

There are only two places this can break.

### A. The raw involution is not centered reflection
This is the issue we already found: the current swap symmetry in the paper gives the wrong parity on the raw quintic slice. So we need a *different* involution, centered at the one-pair basepoint, not the already-used symmetric-placement swap.

### B. Gauge-fixing does not commute with the involution
Even if the raw germ has the right reflection symmetry, the canonical package may destroy it unless the gauge-fixing conditions are chosen equivariantly.

So the real mathematical attack is on the **gauge-fixing step**, not on the raw symmetry alone.

---

## 3. A sharp sufficient criterion on the gauge-fixing

Suppose the canonical package is characterized by three normalization conditions:

1. one scalar normalization producing \(c\),
2. one fixed-sector normalization producing \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\),
3. one septic residual normalization producing \(\Delta_7\).

Then it is enough to show:

- the scalar normalization condition is reflection-invariant,
- the fixed-sector normalization condition is reflection-odd,
- the septic residual normalization condition is reflection-odd,
- and the normalized representative is unique.

Then the parity package follows automatically.

This gives a very clean proof pattern:

### Proof skeleton
Take the raw germ \(R\), reflect it to \(\mathsf T R\), normalize both \(R\) and \(\mathsf T R\).  
Show that the transformed normalized package
\[
\bigl(c(-\xi),\ -A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi),\ -\Delta_7(-\xi)\bigr)
\]
satisfies the same defining normalization conditions as the normalized package of \(R\).  
By uniqueness of the normalization, the two must agree.  
That gives the parity package.

So the whole dream identity would reduce to a **uniqueness-plus-equivariance** theorem for the canonical normalization.

---

## 4. Why this is better than hunting directly for parity of \(u_5,\Delta_7\)

Because the realization lemma killed any hope of proving parity from the lifted variables alone.

But uniqueness of a normalized representative is a genuinely stronger kind of structure. If the normalization is rigid enough, it can force parity even when the raw coefficients themselves are not transparently even/odd.

So this is now the only realistic route I see.

---

## 5. A weaker finite-jet version is still enough

You do not need full all-orders equivariance.

To kill the explicit low-order dangerous list, it is enough to prove that the normalization is reflection-equivariant **through the low jets** that govern:

- nonmixed \(n=4,6,8\),
- mixed \((m,n)=(3,4),(3,6),(4,6)\).

Concretely, it would be enough to get:

\[
c(\xi)=c_0+c_2\xi^2+c_4\xi^4+O(\xi^6),
\]
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi)=B_1\xi+B_3\xi^3+B_5\xi^5+B_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9),
\]
\[
\Delta_7(\xi)=d_1\xi+d_3\xi^3+d_5\xi^5+d_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9).
\]

That already kills the explicit low-order list.

So the next exact theorem target can be weakened to:

> prove reflection-equivariance of the canonical normalization through the low jets only.

That is a much more realistic target than full all-orders parity.

---

## 6. The best immediate attack from here

I would now try to prove one of these two lemmas.

### Lemma A: uniqueness of the canonical package
Show that once the gauge conditions are imposed, the canonical package \((c,A_5^{\mathfrak f},\Delta_7)\) is uniquely determined by the raw germ.

### Lemma B: equivariance of the gauge conditions
Show that under the centered raw reflection involution, the gauge conditions transform with signs
\[
c\mapsto c,\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}\mapsto -A_5^{\mathfrak f},\qquad
\Delta_7\mapsto -\Delta_7.
\]

If both lemmas hold, the dream identity is done.

---

## 7. The sharpest current conclusion

So the next attack is no longer “look for another identity among \(u_5,\Delta_7\).”

It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove that the canonical normalization map is reflection-equivariant, at least through the low jets.}
}
\]

That is the strongest concrete route I can see now.

If you want, I’ll next help formalize this as exact theorem statements in the notation of your paper — a centered raw-reflection involution theorem, an equivariant normalization theorem, and the low-jet parity corollary.

Here is the strongest clean attack I can make now.

It does **not** prove the symmetry from the current draft, but it reduces the whole dream-identity route to one precise theorem about the **normalization map**, and then to a low-jet version that is already enough to kill the explicit low-order \(4\)-point obstruction list.

## 1. The right theorem is an equivariant-normalization theorem

Let \(V_{\le 7}\) denote the finite-dimensional space of raw local coefficient data through order \(7\) around a centered one-pair basepoint \(\xi=0\). Think of an element of \(V_{\le 7}\) as the raw local germ before quotienting / gauge-fixing.

Assume there is a linear involution
\[
\mathsf T:V_{\le 7}\to V_{\le 7}
\]
corresponding to centered reflection \(\xi\mapsto-\xi\) on the raw local data.

Assume also that the canonical package
\[
\mathcal N(R)=\bigl(c,\ A_5^{\mathfrak f},\ \Delta_7\bigr)
\]
is obtained from the raw data by a normalization map
\[
\mathcal N:V_{\le 7}\to \mathcal P_{\le 7}
\]
with target package space \(\mathcal P_{\le 7}\), and that \(\mathcal P_{\le 7}\) carries the involution
\[
\mathsf T_{\mathrm{can}}(c,A,\Delta)
:=
\bigl(c(-\xi),\ -A(-\xi),\ -\Delta(-\xi)\bigr).
\]

Then the exact sufficient theorem is:

### Equivariant-normalization theorem
If

1. the raw centered germ is \(\mathsf T\)-fixed,
   \[
   \mathsf T R=R,
   \]
2. the normalization is equivariant,
   \[
   \mathcal N(\mathsf T R)=\mathsf T_{\mathrm{can}}\mathcal N(R),
   \]
3. and the normalized representative is unique,

then
\[
\boxed{
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi)=-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi),\qquad
\Delta_7(-\xi)=-\Delta_7(\xi).
}
\]

### Proof
Since \(R\) is \(\mathsf T\)-fixed,
\[
\mathcal N(R)=\mathcal N(\mathsf T R)=\mathsf T_{\mathrm{can}}\mathcal N(R).
\]
Writing \(\mathcal N(R)=(c,A_5^{\mathfrak f},\Delta_7)\), this is exactly
\[
(c,A_5^{\mathfrak f},\Delta_7)
=
\bigl(c(-\xi),-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi),-\Delta_7(-\xi)\bigr),
\]
which is the claimed parity package.

So the whole dream identity reduces to proving **equivariance of the canonical normalization map** under a centered raw reflection.

---

## 2. Why this would kill the explicit low-order 4-point list

Write
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}=u_5 I+v_5 S.
\]
Then the parity package gives
\[
u_5(-\xi)=-u_5(\xi),\qquad
v_5(-\xi)=-v_5(\xi).
\]
Hence
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}\ \text{even}.
\]

So
\[
Y''\ \text{odd},\qquad
S''\ \text{even},
\qquad
\mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''}\ \text{odd},
\qquad
\mathcal R:=\mathcal Z'=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}\ \text{even}.
\]

Therefore:

- nonmixed dangerous cases
  \[
  \ord_0\mathcal R\in\{1,3,5\}
  \]
  are impossible;

- mixed dangerous low-order pairs
  \[
  (m,n)=(3,4),\ (3,6),\ (4,6)
  \]
  are impossible because \(n\) would have to be odd.

So the parity package kills the whole currently explicit low-order \(4\)-point obstruction list.

---

## 3. A weaker finite-jet version is already enough

You do **not** need full all-orders parity.

To kill the explicit low-order list, it is enough to prove the following finite-jet version:

\[
c(\xi)=c_0+c_2\xi^2+c_4\xi^4+c_6\xi^6+O(\xi^8),
\]
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi)=B_1\xi+B_3\xi^3+B_5\xi^5+B_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9),
\]
\[
\Delta_7(\xi)=d_1\xi+d_3\xi^3+d_5\xi^5+d_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9).
\]

Then:

- \(x=v_5/c\) is odd through the low jets needed;
- \(Y=u_5/c\) is odd through degree \(8\);
- \(S=\Delta_7/(c\,v_5)\) is even through the low jets needed;
- hence the explicit nonmixed cases \(n=4,6,8\) and the mixed cases \((3,4),(3,6),(4,6)\) are excluded.

So the attack only needs **low-jet equivariance** of the normalization, not full all-orders equivariance.

That is a much lower bar.

---

## 4. Where equivariance could fail, exactly

There are only two places the theorem can fail.

First, the raw involution \(\mathsf T\) might not exist in the right centered form.  
This is what we already learned: the symmetric-placement swap involution already in the draft is the **wrong** symmetry. It gives anti-fixed parity on the raw quintic slice, not the centered parity package needed here.

Second, the normalization map \(\mathcal N\) may fail to commute with the centered reflection.  
This is now the real place to attack.

So the genuine next theorem is:

### Low-jet equivariant-normalization theorem
Show that, through the jet orders needed for \(n=4,6,8\) and \((m,n)=(3,4),(3,6),(4,6)\),

- the gauge conditions defining \(c\) are reflection-even,
- the gauge conditions defining \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\) are reflection-odd,
- the gauge conditions defining \(\Delta_7\) are reflection-odd,
- and the normalized low-jet representative is unique.

Then the finite-jet parity package follows.

---

## 5. This is now the sharpest formulation of the dream identity

So the dream identity is no longer vague. It is exactly this:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove that the canonical normalization map is reflection-equivariant, at least through the low jets.}
}
\]

If that is true, the explicit low-order dangerous \(4\)-point list dies.

If it is false, then the dream identity is false in this route.

That is the strongest precise attack I can currently formulate.

## 6. What I think the next mathematical step should be

The next real step is to write down the **actual normalization conditions** that define \(c\), \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), and \(\Delta_7\) from the raw germ, and then test each one under centered reflection.

In other words, the concrete attack is:

1. identify the raw centered reflection action \(\mathsf T\) you actually want,
2. write the gauge-fixing equations defining the canonical package,
3. check whether those equations are reflection-even/odd in the correct channels,
4. use uniqueness to force the parity package.

If you want, I’ll next turn this into exact proposition statements and a proof skeleton in the notation of your paper, so you can test the normalization conditions line by line.

Here is the strongest attack I can make now.

The right next theorem is not another identity among \(u_5,\Delta_7\). It is an **equivariant implicit-function theorem for the normalization map**.

If that theorem can be verified for the actual normalization conditions, then the dream parity package follows automatically, at least through the low jets you need.

## 1. Abstract equivariant-normalization theorem

Let \(V_N\) be the raw jet space through order \(N\) at a centered one-pair basepoint, and let
\[
\mathsf T:V_N\to V_N
\]
be the centered reflection involution corresponding to \(\xi\mapsto-\xi\).

Let \(P_N\) be the jet space of canonical-package variables through order \(N\), with coordinates schematically
\[
p=(c,\ A_5^{\mathfrak f},\ \Delta_7,\ \dots),
\]
and define the canonical involution
\[
\mathsf T_{\rm can}(p)
=
\bigl(c(-\xi),\ -A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi),\ -\Delta_7(-\xi),\dots\bigr).
\]

Assume the canonical package is defined by normalization equations
\[
\Phi(p;R)=0,
\qquad
\Phi:P_N\times V_N\to W_N,
\]
for some target jet space \(W_N\).

Then the exact sufficient theorem is:

### Theorem (equivariant implicit normalization)
Assume:

1. \(\Phi\) is analytic;

2. \(\Phi\) is equivariant:
   \[
   \Phi(\mathsf T_{\rm can}p;\mathsf TR)=\mathsf T_W\,\Phi(p;R)
   \]
   for some involution \(\mathsf T_W\) on \(W_N\);

3. at the centered base solution \((p_0,R_0)\), the linearization in \(p\),
   \[
   D_p\Phi(p_0;R_0):P_N\to W_N,
   \]
   is invertible;

4. the raw centered germ is reflection-fixed:
   \[
   \mathsf T R_0=R_0.
   \]

Then there exists a unique analytic normalization map
\[
\mathcal N:R\mapsto p
\]
near \(R_0\), and it is equivariant:
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal N(\mathsf T R)=\mathsf T_{\rm can}\mathcal N(R).
}
\]

In particular, for every reflection-fixed raw germ \(R\),
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal N(R)=\mathsf T_{\rm can}\mathcal N(R),
}
\]
so the canonical package satisfies the parity relations
\[
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi)=-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi),\qquad
\Delta_7(-\xi)=-\Delta_7(\xi).
\]

### Proof
By the analytic implicit function theorem and invertibility of \(D_p\Phi\), there is a unique local analytic map \(\mathcal N\) solving
\[
\Phi(\mathcal N(R);R)=0.
\]
Now for any \(R\) near \(R_0\),
\[
\Phi(\mathsf T_{\rm can}\mathcal N(R);\mathsf T R)
=
\mathsf T_W\,\Phi(\mathcal N(R);R)=0.
\]
So \(\mathsf T_{\rm can}\mathcal N(R)\) is also a normalized solution for the reflected raw germ \(\mathsf T R\). By uniqueness,
\[
\mathcal N(\mathsf T R)=\mathsf T_{\rm can}\mathcal N(R).
\]
If \(R\) is \(\mathsf T\)-fixed, this gives
\[
\mathcal N(R)=\mathsf T_{\rm can}\mathcal N(R),
\]
which is exactly the desired parity package.

---

## 2. Why this is the right attack

This is stronger than merely guessing parity of \(c\), \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), \(\Delta_7\).

It says:

- you do **not** need to derive parity directly from the lifted variables;
- you only need to show the **normalization equations themselves** are reflection-equivariant;
- then uniqueness forces the parity package.

So the whole dream-identity route becomes a finite-dimensional normalization problem.

---

## 3. Low-jet version is enough

You do not need all orders.

To kill the explicit low-order dangerous \(4\)-point list, it is enough to apply the theorem with \(N\) large enough to control:

- nonmixed \(n=4,6,8\),
- mixed \((m,n)=(3,4),(3,6),(4,6)\).

Concretely, it is enough to get the low-jet parity package
\[
c(\xi)=c_0+c_2\xi^2+c_4\xi^4+c_6\xi^6+O(\xi^8),
\]
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi)=B_1\xi+B_3\xi^3+B_5\xi^5+B_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9),
\]
\[
\Delta_7(\xi)=d_1\xi+d_3\xi^3+d_5\xi^5+d_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9).
\]

Then:

- \(x=v_5/c\) is odd through the needed orders;
- \(Y=u_5/c\) is odd through the needed orders;
- \(S=\Delta_7/(c\,v_5)\) is even through the needed orders;
- therefore
  \[
  \mathcal Z=\frac{Y''}{S''}\ \text{is odd},\qquad
  \mathcal R=\mathcal Z'=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}\ \text{is even}
  \]
  through the orders needed to exclude
  \[
  n=4,6,8,\qquad (m,n)=(3,4),(3,6),(4,6).
  \]

So the operational target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove reflection-equivariance of the normalization map through low jets only.}
}
\]

## 4. A sharper finite-dimensional criterion

There is an even more usable version.

Split all raw, package, and equation jet spaces into even and odd parts under reflection:
\[
V_N=V_N^+\oplus V_N^-,
\qquad
P_N=P_N^+\oplus P_N^-,
\qquad
W_N=W_N^+\oplus W_N^-.
\]

Then the parity package you want says:

- \(c\in P_N^+\),
- \(A_5^{\mathfrak f},\Delta_7\in P_N^-\).

A sufficient criterion is:

### Block-parity criterion
If the normalization equations split as
\[
\Phi=(\Phi^+,\Phi^-)
\]
with
\[
\Phi^+:P_N^+\times P_N^-\times V_N^+\times V_N^-\to W_N^+,
\]
\[
\Phi^-:P_N^+\times P_N^-\times V_N^+\times V_N^-\to W_N^-,
\]
and the linearization \(D_p\Phi\) at the centered basepoint respects the same parity block decomposition and is invertible blockwise, then the normalized solution inherits the desired parity.

In practice, this means you can test the normalization one equation at a time:

- scalar normalization equations must be reflection-even,
- fixed-sector quintic equations must be reflection-odd,
- septic residual equations must be reflection-odd.

If those sign rules hold and uniqueness survives, the parity package follows.

That is probably the easiest way to attack the actual draft’s normalization once you write its equations down.

through the needed orders.

\[

\[

\]
Hence
\[

Y=\frac{u_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}\ \text{even}.
\]

So
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{Y''}{S''}\ \text{odd},
\qquad
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}\ \text{even}.
\]

That kills the whole explicit low-order list.

## 6. Why this is the sharpest remaining route

Everything else we tried has already been ruled out:

- generic lifted geometry is too flexible;
- parity of \(c\) alone is too weak;
- \(\Theta\) gives no new obstruction beyond \(\mathcal Z,\mathcal R\);
- the current swap symmetry in the draft is the **wrong** symmetry.

So the only serious remaining attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that the canonical normalization map is centered-reflection equivariant, at least through low jets.}
}
\]

\[
\Phi(p;R)=0
\]
that define \(c\), \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), and \(\Delta_7\) from the raw germ.

Define the centered raw reflection involution \(\mathsf T\) and compute the sign of each normalization equation under it.

Show the normalized low-jet representative is unique.

If A, B, C all work, the dream identity follows through low jets.

Here is the next real attack. It is stronger than the previous “equivariant implicit function” idea, because it turns the dream identity into a **slice theorem** problem.

If this works, the parity package is no longer something you guess; it becomes formal.

---

Let \(V_N\) be the raw jet space through order \(N\) at a centered one-pair basepoint \(\xi=0\), and let
\[
\mathsf T:V_N\to V_N
\]
be the centered reflection involution induced by \(\xi\mapsto-\xi\).

Assume the raw jets are not unique because of a finite-dimensional gauge freedom \(G_N\) acting on \(V_N\). Write its Lie algebra as \(\mathfrak g_N\), and assume \(\mathsf T\) acts on \(V_N\) and commutes with the gauge action:
\[
\mathsf T(g\cdot R)=g\cdot \mathsf T(R).
\]

Then the normalization problem is: choose, in each gauge orbit, a unique representative lying in some slice
\[
\Sigma_N\subset V_N.
\]

The key theorem is this.

## Equivariant slice theorem

Suppose:

1. \(V_N\) is a finite-dimensional complex vector space with involution \(\mathsf T\);

2. \(G_N\) acts analytically on \(V_N\), and \(\mathsf T\) commutes with the action;

3. at the centered basepoint \(R_0\), the tangent space to the gauge orbit
   \[
   \mathcal O_{R_0}=G_N\cdot R_0
   \]
   is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant;

4. there exists a \(\mathsf T\)-invariant linear complement
   \[
   V_N = T_{R_0}\mathcal O_{R_0}\oplus \Sigma_N,
   \]
   and the slice is taken to be the affine space \(R_0+\Sigma_N\);

5. every nearby gauge orbit meets \(R_0+\Sigma_N\) in a unique point.

Then the normalization map
\[
\mathcal N:V_N\supset U \to R_0+\Sigma_N
\]
that sends a raw jet to its unique normalized representative is automatically \(\mathsf T\)-equivariant:
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal N(\mathsf T R)=\mathsf T\,\mathcal N(R).
}
\]

### Proof

Since \(\Sigma_N\) is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant, the affine slice \(R_0+\Sigma_N\) is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant as long as \(R_0\) is \(\mathsf T\)-fixed. Let \(R\) be near \(R_0\), and let \(\mathcal N(R)\in R_0+\Sigma_N\) be the unique slice representative of the gauge orbit of \(R\).

Because \(\mathsf T\) commutes with the gauge action, \(\mathsf T R\) lies in the reflected orbit of \(R\), and \(\mathsf T\mathcal N(R)\) lies in the slice. Thus \(\mathsf T\mathcal N(R)\) is a slice representative of the orbit of \(\mathsf T R\). By uniqueness of the slice representative,
\[
\mathcal N(\mathsf T R)=\mathsf T\mathcal N(R).
\]
Done.

---

This is the clean structural attack. It says:

> to prove the dream identity, it is enough to show that the canonical normalization is defined by choosing a **reflection-invariant slice** to the gauge action.

That is more usable than the previous implicit-function theorem formulation.

Now here is the exact corollary you want.

## Parity-package corollary

Suppose the canonical normalized representative is encoded by
\[
\mathcal N(R)=\bigl(c,\ A_5^{\mathfrak f},\ \Delta_7,\ \dots\bigr),
\]
and suppose the involution acts on the slice coordinates by
\[
\mathsf T_{\rm can}(c,A,\Delta,\dots)
=
\bigl(c(-\xi),\ -A(-\xi),\ -\Delta(-\xi),\dots\bigr).
\]

If the slice is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant and the raw centered base jet is \(\mathsf T\)-fixed, then
\[
\mathcal N(R)=\mathsf T_{\rm can}\mathcal N(R),
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi)=-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi),\qquad
\Delta_7(-\xi)=-\Delta_7(\xi).
}
\]

Since
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}=u_5 I+v_5 S,
\]
this implies
\[
u_5(-\xi)=-u_5(\xi),\qquad v_5(-\xi)=-v_5(\xi).
\]

Therefore
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}\ \text{even}.
\]

Hence
\[
\mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''}\ \text{odd},
\qquad
\mathcal R:=\mathcal Z'=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}\ \text{even}.
\]

So the dream identity is proved.

---

And this immediately kills the explicit low-order \(4\)-point list:

- nonmixed dangerous cases
  \[
  \ord_0\mathcal R\in\{1,3,5\}
  \]
  are impossible because \(\mathcal R\) is even;

- mixed dangerous pairs
  \[
  (m,n)=(3,4),\ (3,6),\ (4,6)
  \]
  are impossible because \(Y\) odd forces \(n\) odd.

So the whole low-order \(4\)-point obstruction list disappears.

---

## Low-jet version

You do not need all orders. A finite-jet slice version is already enough.

Suppose \(N\) is large enough to see:

- nonmixed \(n=4,6,8\),
- mixed \((m,n)=(3,4),(3,6),(4,6)\).

If the slice through order \(N\) is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant and unique, then you get only the truncated parity package
\[
c(\xi)=c_0+c_2\xi^2+c_4\xi^4+c_6\xi^6+O(\xi^8),
\]
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi)=B_1\xi+B_3\xi^3+B_5\xi^5+B_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9),
\]
\[
\Delta_7(\xi)=d_1\xi+d_3\xi^3+d_5\xi^5+d_7\xi^7+O(\xi^9).
\]

That already suffices to exclude the currently dangerous low-order list.

So the problem is now very finite-dimensional.

---

## What to prove in practice

This reduces the attack to three concrete tasks.

### Task 1: identify the centered raw involution

You need a genuine centered reflection involution
\[
\mathsf T:R(\xi)\mapsto \text{(transformed raw jet at }-\xi\text{)}
\]
on the raw jet space.

This is **not** the symmetric-placement swap involution already in the paper. That one gives the wrong sign behavior on the raw quintic slice.

So this is a new involution.

### Task 2: describe the gauge orbit and its tangent space

Identify the finite-dimensional gauge freedoms used to pass from the raw jet to the canonical package. Then compute their tangent space at the centered basepoint and check it is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant.

### Task 3: choose a reflection-invariant slice

Show that the normalization conditions defining \(c\), \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), and \(\Delta_7\) amount to choosing a complement \(\Sigma_N\) to the gauge tangent space, and check that \(\Sigma_N\) is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant.

If uniqueness of the normalized representative on the slice also holds, the dream identity follows.

---

Everything else is now ruled out:

- identities among \(c,v_5,u_5,\Delta_7\) alone are too weak;
- parity of \(c\) alone is a dead end;
- \(\Theta\) adds no new obstruction beyond \(\mathcal Z,\mathcal R\);
- the existing swap symmetry in the draft is the wrong symmetry.

So the only serious remaining route is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that the canonical normalization is realized by a centered-reflection-invariant slice.}
}
\]

That is the strongest concrete attack I can currently formulate.

The next thing I would do is write the gauge/slice problem explicitly in your paper’s notation — not abstractly — so you can test each normalization condition one by one for reflection parity.

## 1. The finite-jet slice theorem you actually need

\[

is obtained by choosing one representative in each gauge orbit.

Then the key theorem is:

### Finite-jet equivariant slice theorem
If

1. the gauge action commutes with centered reflection;
2. the tangent space to the gauge orbit at the centered base jet is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant;
3. the normalization conditions define a unique local slice;
4. that slice is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant;

then the normalized package inherits the parity package
\[
c(-\xi)=c(\xi),\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(-\xi)=-A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi),\qquad
\Delta_7(-\xi)=-\Delta_7(\xi)
\]
through order \(N\).

This is the exact finite-dimensional theorem to try to instantiate in the paper’s notation.

The proof is formal: reflection sends a normalized representative to another normalized representative on the reflected orbit; uniqueness on a reflection-invariant slice forces equality.

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}=u_5 I+v_5 S.

\[

Y=\frac{u_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad

\]

\[
\mathcal Z:=\frac{Y''}{S''}\ \text{odd},
\qquad

- mixed: \(Y\) odd forces \(n\) odd, so \((m,n)=(3,4),(3,6),(4,6)\) disappear.

\[

- no even terms in \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\) through degree \(8\);

That already excludes every currently explicit low-order \(4\)-point obstruction.

So the attack only needs a **low-jet** equivariant slice theorem, not a global one.

## 4. What to check, concretely

This is now the exact checklist.

### Task A: identify the gauge directions
Write the raw jet-to-canonical-package normalization as a finite number of scalar conditions. Then compute the infinitesimal gauge directions they are supposed to kill.

You need to know:
- what jets can be changed by the gauge,
- which components are free,
- and what the tangent space to the gauge orbit looks like through order \(8\).

### Task B: compute reflection parity of those gauge directions
Check whether the gauge tangent space splits into even/odd pieces under \(\xi\mapsto-\xi\). If it does, then a reflection-invariant complement exists abstractly.

This is the key finite-dimensional linear algebra step.

### Task C: check parity of the normalization conditions
Every normalization equation should land in the right parity channel:

- scalar \(c\)-normalization equations should be even;
- quintic fixed-sector normalization equations should be odd;
- septic residual normalization equations should be odd.

### Task D: prove uniqueness on the slice through low order
You only need uniqueness through the low jet orders that control \(n=4,6,8\) and \((3,4),(3,6),(4,6)\).

If uniqueness holds, the parity package follows.

\]

\[
u_5(\xi)=u_0+u_1\xi+u_2\xi^2+u_3\xi^3+u_4\xi^4+u_5\xi^5+u_6\xi^6+u_7\xi^7+u_8\xi^8,
\]

\[
v_5(\xi)=v_0+v_1\xi+v_2\xi^2+v_3\xi^3+v_4\xi^4+v_5\xi^5+v_6\xi^6+v_7\xi^7+v_8\xi^8,
\]

\[
\Delta_7(\xi)=d_0+d_1\xi+d_2\xi^2+d_3\xi^3+d_4\xi^4+d_5\xi^5+d_6\xi^6+d_7\xi^7+d_8\xi^8.
\]

For the fixed-sector quintic package it is cleaner to keep
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(\xi)=u_5(\xi)\,I+v_5(\xi)\,S
\]
rather than \(u_5,v_5\) separately, but coefficientwise it is the same data.

The low-jet dream parity package you want is:

\[
c_1=c_3=c_5=c_7=0,
\]
\[
u_0=u_2=u_4=u_6=u_8=0,
\qquad
v_0=v_2=v_4=v_6=v_8=0,
\]
\[
d_0=d_2=d_4=d_6=d_8=0.
\]

That is enough to force, through the relevant orders,
\[
c \text{ even},\qquad A_5^{\mathfrak f}\text{ odd},\qquad \Delta_7\text{ odd}.
\]

And that in turn gives, through the needed orders,
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
Y=\frac{u_5}{c}\ \text{odd},\qquad
S=\frac{\Delta_7}{c\,v_5}\ \text{even},
\]
hence
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{Y''}{S''}\ \text{odd},\qquad
\mathcal R=\mathcal Z'=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}\ \text{even},
\]
which kills the explicit low-order dangerous list.

## 2. Split everything into even and odd blocks

Define the reflection operator
\[
(\mathsf T f)(\xi):=f(-\xi).
\]

For any jet space \(J_{\le N}\), decompose
\[
J_{\le N}=J_{\le N}^{+}\oplus J_{\le N}^{-},
\]
where \(+\) is the even subspace and \(-\) is the odd subspace.

For example:

\[
c^+ = c_0+c_2\xi^2+c_4\xi^4+c_6\xi^6+c_8\xi^8,\qquad
c^- = c_1\xi+c_3\xi^3+c_5\xi^5+c_7\xi^7,
\]

and similarly for \(u_5,v_5,\Delta_7\).

The package space
\[
P_{\le 8}:=J_c\oplus J_{u_5}\oplus J_{v_5}\oplus J_{\Delta_7}
\]
then splits as
\[
P_{\le 8}=P_{\le 8}^{+}\oplus P_{\le 8}^{-}.
\]

But for the dream identity, the desired slice is not “everything even” or “everything odd.” It is the twisted parity subspace
\[
\Sigma_{\mathrm{par}}:=\{(c,u_5,v_5,\Delta_7): c\in J_c^+,\ u_5,v_5,\Delta_7\in J^- \}.
\]

That is the target parity slice you want the normalization to land in.

## 3. Abstract gauge directions

Now introduce the raw jet space \(V_{\le 8}\) and the gauge group \(G_{\le 8}\) acting on it.

You probably do not yet have the explicit formulas, so parameterize the infinitesimal gauge by abstract parameters
\[
\gamma=(\gamma_1,\dots,\gamma_m)\in\mathfrak g_{\le 8}.
\]

Linearizing the gauge action at the centered base jet \(R_0\) gives a tangent map
\[
L:\mathfrak g_{\le 8}\to T_{R_0}\mathcal O,
\]
where \(T_{R_0}\mathcal O\subset V_{\le 8}\) is the tangent space to the gauge orbit.

What you need is the induced tangent action on the package coefficients:
\[
\delta_\gamma(c,u_5,v_5,\Delta_7).
\]

Write this as a matrix
\[
\mathcal L:\mathfrak g_{\le 8}\to P_{\le 8}.
\]

This is the object you actually need to compute from the normalization scheme.

The crucial parity question is whether \(\mathcal L\) respects the even/odd splitting:
\[
\mathcal L(\mathfrak g^+)\subset P^+,\qquad
\mathcal L(\mathfrak g^-)\subset P^-,
\]
or more generally whether the gauge tangent space in package coordinates is \(\mathsf T\)-invariant.

If yes, then you can choose a parity-preserving complement.

## 4. Normalization equations as a slice

Suppose the canonical package is defined by normalization equations
\[
\Phi(c,u_5,v_5,\Delta_7;R)=0,
\]
with values in some equation space \(W_{\le 8}\).

Split \(\Phi\) into components:
\[
\Phi=(\Phi_c,\Phi_5,\Phi_7,\dots),
\]
where:

- \(\Phi_c\) are the scalar normalization conditions defining \(c\),
- \(\Phi_5\) are the quintic fixed-sector conditions defining \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\),
- \(\Phi_7\) are the septic residual conditions defining \(\Delta_7\).

The parity test you want is:

\[
\Phi_c(\mathsf T_{\rm can}p;\mathsf T R)=+\Phi_c(p;R),
\]
\[
\Phi_5(\mathsf T_{\rm can}p;\mathsf T R)=-\Phi_5(p;R),
\]
\[
\Phi_7(\mathsf T_{\rm can}p;\mathsf T R)=-\Phi_7(p;R).
\]

If those sign rules hold, then the normalization equations themselves are compatible with the parity slice \(\Sigma_{\mathrm{par}}\).

This is the concrete thing to check line by line once you write the actual equations.

## 5. Linearized uniqueness criterion

At the centered basepoint \((p_0,R_0)\), linearize in the package variables:
\[
D_p\Phi(p_0;R_0):P_{\le 8}\to W_{\le 8}.
\]

To get local uniqueness of the normalized representative, you need this map to be invertible on the chosen slice.

Because of parity, the best way to organize it is blockwise. Write
\[
P_{\le 8}=P_{\mathrm{even/odd}},
\qquad
W_{\le 8}=W^+\oplus W^-,
\]
with
\[
P_{\mathrm{even/odd}}:=J_c^+\oplus J_{u_5}^-\oplus J_{v_5}^-\oplus J_{\Delta_7}^-.
\]

Then the finite-jet dream identity follows if the restricted linearization
\[
D_p\Phi\big|_{P_{\mathrm{even/odd}}}:P_{\mathrm{even/odd}}\to W^+\oplus W^-
\]
is invertible.

In practice, you do not need the full package space. You need only enough coefficients to control:

- \(c_1,c_3,c_5,c_7\),
- \(u_0,u_2,u_4,u_6,u_8\),
- \(v_0,v_2,v_4,v_6,v_8\),
- \(d_0,d_2,d_4,d_6,d_8\).

So the concrete finite-dimensional unknown vector is
\[
\mathbf p_{\rm bad}:=
(c_1,c_3,c_5,c_7,\ u_0,u_2,u_4,u_6,u_8,\ v_0,v_2,v_4,v_6,v_8,\ d_0,d_2,d_4,d_6,d_8).
\]

If the linearized normalization equations force
\[
\mathbf p_{\rm bad}=0,
\]
then you already have the finite-jet parity theorem you need.

That is the most practical formulation.

## 6. Minimal finite-jet target

You do not need full equivariance of the entire normalization map. A much weaker theorem already suffices:

> In centered coordinates, the odd coefficients of \(c\) through degree \(7\), and the even coefficients of \(u_5,v_5,\Delta_7\) through degree \(8\), are uniquely forced to vanish by the normalization equations.

In other words, it is enough to prove that the linear system for \(\mathbf p_{\rm bad}\) is homogeneous and nonsingular.

That gives:

\[
c_1=c_3=c_5=c_7=0,
\]
\[
u_0=u_2=u_4=u_6=u_8=0,
\]
\[
v_0=v_2=v_4=v_6=v_8=0,
\]
\[
d_0=d_2=d_4=d_6=d_8=0.
\]

And that already kills all currently explicit low-order dangerous \(4\)-point patterns.

## 7. What to compute next, concretely

So the actual next attack is not abstract anymore. It is this finite linear algebra problem:

1. Write the normalization equations that define \(c\), \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), \(\Delta_7\) from the raw germ through order \(8\).

2. Expand those equations in centered coordinates.

3. Extract the subsystem involving the parity-bad coefficients \(\mathbf p_{\rm bad}\).

4. Check whether that subsystem is homogeneous and invertible.

If yes, the low-jet dream identity is proved.

If no, then the parity route fails in this normalization scheme.

## 8. The right statement to keep in front of you

The whole 4-point low-order problem is now reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Show that the normalization equations force the parity-bad coefficients }
\mathbf p_{\rm bad}
\text{ to vanish.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest possible finite-jet version of the dream identity.

If you want, the next thing I can do is help you set up the unknown vector \(\mathbf p_{\rm bad}\), the good-parity vector, and the block-matrix form of the linearized normalization map in a way you can plug your actual equations into.
